Defining Shart: Distinction from Cause and Defense of Attributes
Defends the definition of shart against a theological objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi definition of shart from sabab and apply the muqtada/mu'aththir distinction to defend the definition against objections involving divine attributes.

Core Idea
A condition (shart) depends on something else but is not a cause and does not require a voluntary agent.

In Hanafi usul, the definition of 'condition' (shart) is precise: it is something upon which the existence of another thing depends, yet remains distinct from both the effect and the cause. This lesson unpacks that definition through a challenging theological objection regarding God's eternal attributes. By parsing the rebuttal, you will see how Hanafi scholars distinguish between a 'determinant' (muqtada) and a 'voluntary agent' (mu'aththir), a key nuance that prevents logical pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition of Shart
Locate the definition being defended: shart is 'that upon which effect depends, while being distinct from the agent'. Note that it excludes the cause (sabab) because the cause directly necessitates the effect.
The Hanafi definition emphasizes the dependency relation and the separation from the agent.
2
Understand the Objection
The objection argues: If shart is distinct from the agent, then God's eternal attributes (like life as a condition for knowledge) would require an agent, leading to infinite regress or multiple necessary beings. Grasp why this is a challenge.
The objector equates 'agent' with 'determinant', which the rebuttal will later distinguish.
3
Follow the First Rebuttal (Ibn Sina's View)
The first response states that attributes exist 'for the subject', not independently. Their existence is by the essence, not by themselves, so no multiplicity of independent necessary beings.
This counters the charge of multiple necessary beings but does not fully resolve the need for an agent.
4
Apply the Second Rebuttal: Determinant vs. Agent
The core distinction: 'determinant' (muqtada) brings existence, while 'agent' (mu'aththir) brings existence by will and choice. Attributes need the essence as a determinant, not as a voluntary agent. Thus, no voluntary agent is required, and the objection fails.
This is the key analytical move that saves the definition.
5
Connect to the Broader Usul Principle
See how this distinction informs fiqh: conditions (like purity for prayer) are not causes; they are prerequisites that do not produce the effect by themselves. The ruling depends on the presence of the condition, not on an agent.
This clarifies why missing a condition invalidates an act without requiring a corrective cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودفع بأن المتبادر من الحد (كونه) أي ما يتوقف عليه التأثير (مغاير للمؤثر) فيخرج السبب ثم أورد على عكسه الحياة في العلم القديم فإنها شرط لوجود العلم له تعالى واتصافه به ولا تأثير للمؤثر فيه إذ المحوج إلى المؤثر (الحدوث عند جمهور المتكلمين، وعلمه تعالى قديم وهذا لا يرد على من جعل العلة للحاجة الامكان كما عليه المحققون من المتكلمين ومنهم المعرّف بهذا التعريف قيل: لو تم هذا أي المحوج الحدوث (لكانت صفات الواجب تعالى مجده (وهي زائدة قديمة) لامتناع قيام الحوادث (مستغنية عن المؤثر حتى عن الذات الموصوفة بهذا إذ لا حدوث فلا حاجة (فيلزم إما كونها واجب الوجود إن كان الوجود ضرورياً لها بالنظر إلى ذواتها فيتعدد الواجب بالذات العياذ بالله (أو كونها ممكنة مستغنية عن المؤثر إن كان ممكن الوجود (وحينئذ يلزم انسداد باب إثبات الله تعالى أي العلم به فإن مداره على حاجة الممكن إلى المؤثر، وقد جوزتم وجوده بلا مؤثر (أقول : أولاً : وجود الصفة هو وجودها لموصوفها على ما صرح به ابن سينا) فوجود صفاته له تعالى قائم بذاته تعالى لا بأنفسها فلا يلزم وجوب وجود موجودات) متعددة (مستقلة وإنما المحال (ذلك ظاهر هذا الكلام يقتضي جواز واجبين غير مستقلي الذات، ولا يجترىء على التفوه به مسلم بل عاقل فضلاً عن تجويزه، ثم إن الصفات قديمة البتة، وإذ علة الحاجة الحدوث عندهم فلا حاجة، فإما ممكنات فيلزم وجود متساوي النسبة وإلى العدم من غير مرجح بقي كونها واجبة والوجوب ينافي الحاجة في الوجود فتكون مستغنية عن الذات، فتكون مستقلة فيلزم ما سلم استحالته ويلزم خلاف المفروض أيضاً من بطلان كونها صفات ويمكن أن يقرر كلام المصنف بأن الصفات وجودها ليس وجودها في أنفسها بل لموصوفاتها وهي واجبة بالقياس إلى الذات وكل ما هو واجب بشيء فهو واجب به لا بالذات فلا وجوب بالذات ولا استغناء والمحال هو الثاني، وهو ملزوم الاستقلال، وعبر بوجوب موجودات مستقلة عن الواجب بالذات تعبيراً عن الشيء بلازمه فهذا إما معارضة أو نقض إجمالي، ولا ينقطع بهما مادة الشبهة ولذا أردف بالثاني المشتمل على الحل، هذا غاية التوجيه لكلامه (و) أقول ((ثانياً) إنها ممكنة مستغنية عن المؤثر لكنها محتاجة إلى المقتضى و (إنما يلزم الانسداد الباب العلم بالصانع بالآيات لو كانت مستغنية عن المقتضى) مطلقاً (والمؤثر عندهم أخص منه فإن المفيد للوجود يقال له المقتضى، فإن كان مفيداً بالإرادة والاختيار يسمى مؤثراً، فالصفات ممكنة محتاجة في وجوداتها إلى الذات الموصوفة بها لكن الذات جاعلة إياها بالإيجاب لا بالاختيار وإلا لزم التسلسل، وكيف يجوز أن الاختيار صدر بالاختيار أو العلم صدر به بعد العلم وإذا كانت مجعولة بالإيجاب لم يحتج إلى المؤثر، ثم هذا موقوف على ما حقق الإمام فخر الدين الرازي رحمه الله في بعض كتبه الكلامية أن المراد بقولهم: المحوج هو الحدوث لا الإمكان أن المحوج إلى الجاعل الخلق بالاختيار هو الحدوث لا الإمكان (فافهم)
And it is rebutted that what is understood from the definition is that it (i.e., that upon which effect depends) is distinct from the agent, so the cause is excluded. Then an objection was raised against its converse: life in eternal knowledge is a condition for the existence of knowledge for Him, may He be exalted, and His being described with it, and the agent has no effect on it, because what necessitates the agent is origination according to the majority of theologians, and His knowledge is eternal. And this does not apply to one who makes the cause need for possibility, as held by the verifying scholars among the theologians, including the one who defined with this definition. It was said: If this is sound, i.e., that what necessitates is origination, then the attributes of the Necessary Existent, may His glory be exalted (and they are superadded and eternal), due to the impossibility of accidents subsisting, would be independent of the agent, even of the essence described with this, since there is no origination, thus no need. Then either they must be necessary of existence if existence is necessary for them in themselves, leading to multiple necessary beings per se (God forbid), or they must be possible but independent of the agent if they are possible existence. Then it would necessitate closing the door of proving God, i.e., knowledge of Him, because it is based on the need of the possible for the agent, and you have permitted existence without an agent. I say: First, the existence of an attribute is its existence for its subject, as stated by Ibn Sina. So the existence of His attributes is subsistent by His essence, not by themselves; thus it does not necessitate the existence of multiple independent existents. The impossible is rather that this apparent speech implies the permissibility of two non-independent necessary beings, and no Muslim, let alone a rational person, would dare utter that, let alone permit it. Then, the attributes are absolutely eternal, and since the cause of need is origination according to them, there is no need. So either they are possible, leading to an equal relation to existence and non-existence without a preponderant; or they are necessary, and necessity negates need in existence, so they become independent of the essence, thus independent, leading to what he admitted as impossible, and also contradicting the assumption that they are attributes. The author's statement can be construed: attributes do not have existence in themselves but for their subjects; they are necessary in relation to the essence, and whatever is necessary due to something is necessary by it, not by essence, so there is no necessity by essence nor independence, and the impossible is the second, which entails independence. He expressed 'necessity of independent existents separate from the Necessary by essence' as an expression of the thing by its necessary consequence. So this is either a confrontation or a summary refutation, but the material of doubt is not cut off by them, so he appended a second reply containing the solution. This is the utmost direction of his statement. And I say, secondly: They are possible, independent of the agent, but they need the determinant, and the closing of the door of proving the Maker by signs would only follow if they were independent of the determinant absolutely. And 'agent' for them is more specific than 'determinant'; for the one that brings existence is called a determinant, and if it brings existence by will and choice, it is called an agent. So the attributes are possible, needing in their existence the essence described by them, but the essence makes them by necessitation, not by choice, otherwise it would lead to infinite regress. How could choice originate by choice or knowledge originate after knowledge? And if they are made by necessitation, they do not need the agent. Then this depends on what Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, may God have mercy on him, verified in some of his theological books: that what they mean by 'what necessitates is origination, not possibility' is that what necessitates the maker's creation by choice is origination, not possibility. So understand.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a dense theological-legal discussion defending the definition of 'condition' (shart) in Hanafi usul. The author rebuts an objection about the attributes of God, arguing that conditions are distinct from causes and do not require a voluntary agent. The key move is distinguishing between a 'determinant' (muqtada) and an 'agent' (mu'aththir): attributes are dependent on the essence by necessitation, not by choice, so the objection fails. The passage illustrates the intersection of kalam and usul in Hanafi thought.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): That upon which the existence of something depends, while being distinct from the agent and not part of the effect.
المقتضى (al-muqtada): That which brings existence (generic), whether by will or by necessitation.
المؤثر (al-mu'aththir): An agent that brings about existence by will and choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key distinction that saves the definition of shart from the objection about God's attributes?
A) Attributes are not conditions.
B) Attributes need a determinant (muqtada), not a voluntary agent (mu'aththir).
C) Attributes are part of the essence.
D) Attributes are caused by the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of shart is that upon which something depends, distinct from the cause, and does not require a voluntary agent; this distinction resolves theological objections and clarifies fiqh rulings.

Classification of Shart and Its Logical Implications
Defining the three types of shart and their logical combinations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of shart (rational, legal, linguistic) and apply the nine-fold classification of condition-result combinations in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Shart is defined by its source: intellect, law, or language; its logical relation to the result determines legal entailments.

In usul al-fiqh, understanding the concept of 'condition' (shart) is fundamental to interpreting legal texts. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defines shart in three ways—rational, legal, and linguistic—and explains how conditions relate to their results in conditional statements. The author also refines the linguistic definition by distinguishing it from the grammatical conditional particle. Mastering these categories is essential for analysing conditional formulations in the Qur'an and prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of shart
Read the opening lines: 'Then the aforementioned condition: it is rational... legal... linguistic.' Note the three categories and their definitions: rational (e.g., substance for accident), legal (e.g., purity for prayer), linguistic (as 'sign').
The linguistic shart is not a separate usul category but refers to the sign-making function.
2
Understand the refutation of Ibn al-Hajib
The author explicitly rejects Ibn al-Hajib's claim that linguistic shart is a valid category and that it is the operand of 'in' and its sisters. Instead, the grammarians' usage is derivative: the operand becomes a marker for the result.
This clarifies that the term 'shart' in usul is not identical to the grammatical conditional.
3
Analyse the logical entailment
The passage explains that when a condition is a necessitating cause, its existence entails the existence of the result. However, its negation does not entail the negation of the result because the result might arise from another cause. This is a key principle for interpreting legal conditionals.
Yes: if condition and result are equivalent, negation does entail negation.
4
Enumerate the nine combinations
Both condition and result can be single, multiple collectively, or multiple alternatively. Multiply three by three to get nine possible relations. Understand how each affects entailment.
This classification is used to analyse complex conditional statements in fiqh texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هو أي الشرط المذكور عقلي يحكم بشرطيته العقل كالجوهر للعرض فإن العرض لا يوجد بدون الجوهر وشرعي يحكم بشرطيته الشرع كالطهارة للصلاة فإنها لا توجد دونها وأما الشرط لغة فهو العلامة ومنه أشراط الساعة أي علاماتها فيه إشارة إلى أن الشرط اللغوي لا يصلح قسماً منه كما زعم ابن الحاجب وإلى أن الشرط اللغوي العلامة لا مدخول أن وأخواتها كما زعمه أيضاً وأما تسمية النحاة مدخول أن وأخواتها شرطاً فلصيرورته علامة على الجزاء هذا وجه التسمية إذ كثيراً ما يستعمل إن فيما لا يتوقف المسبب بعده على غيره فهو علة موجبة فيستلزم وجوده لوجوده أي وجود مدخول أن وجود المسبب فهو علامة على الجزاء لا نفيه لنفيه أي لا يستلزم نفي مدخول أن نفي المسبب لاحتمال أن يوجد من سبب آخر بأن يكون المسبب أعم منه ، ويكون لازماً له ولسبب آخر ولا يلزم توارد السببين على أثر واحد بالشخص لأنه لعمومه لا يكون واحداً شخصياً، نعم : إذا كان مساوياً له يلزم من نفيه نفيه ولهذا ينتج في الاستثنائي أي المتصل وضع المقدم منه لوضع التالي منه لا نفيه لنفيه ينتج نفي المقدم لنفي التالي وهو أي الشرط قد يتحد وقد يتعدد جمعاً بأن يكون الشرط المجموع من حيث المجموع أو يتعدد بدلاً بأن يكون الشرط واحداً لا بعينه من أمور متعددة فهذه ثلاثة من الأقسام وكذا الجزاء قد يتحد وقد يتعدد جمعاً وقد يتعدد بدلاً فالمجموع تسعة حاصلة من ضرب الثلاثة في الثلاثة
Then the aforementioned condition: it is rational—the intellect judges its conditionality like substance for accident, for the accident does not exist without substance; and legal—the Law judges its conditionality like purity for prayer, for it does not exist without it. As for the condition linguistically, it is the sign, from which are the signs of the Hour, i.e., its markers. In this there is an indication that the linguistic condition is not valid as a category of it as Ibn al-Hajib claimed, and that the linguistic condition is the sign, not the operand of 'in' and its sisters as he also claimed. As for the grammarians' naming the operand of 'in' and its sisters a condition, it is because it becomes a marker for the result—this is the basis of the naming—since often 'in' is used where the caused does not depend on something else afterward, so it is a necessitating cause: its existence entails the existence of the result, i.e., the existence of the operand of 'in' entails the existence of the result, so it is a marker for the result; its negation does not entail the negation of the result because it is possible that it exists from another cause, meaning the result is more general than it, being necessary for it and for another cause, and the concurrence of two causes on a single effect in person is not necessary because due to its generality it is not a single individual. Yes: if it is equivalent to it, then its negation entails its negation. That is why in the connected exceptive syllogism, positing the antecedent entails positing the consequent, not negating its negation; negating the antecedent entails negating the consequent. And the condition: it may be single or multiple collectively, meaning the condition is the sum as a whole, or multiple alternatively, meaning the condition is one unspecified from multiple things. These are three categories. Likewise, the result may be single or multiple collectively or multiple alternatively. So the total is nine, resulting from multiplying three by three.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term shart (condition) as used in usul al-fiqh. The commentator explains that shart has three aspects: rational (e.g., substance for accident), legal (e.g., purity for prayer), and linguistic (meaning 'sign'). He refutes Ibn al-Hajib's claim that the linguistic condition is a separate category of shart in usul, and also refutes the notion that the linguistic condition is the grammatical particle 'in' and its sisters; rather, the grammarians call the operand a condition because it serves as a marker for the result. The passage then analyzes the logical relationship between condition and result: existence of condition entails existence of result (if the result is specific to it), but non-existence of condition does not entail non-existence of result if the result can arise from another cause. This is exemplified in the exceptive syllogism. Finally, the passage enumerates the combinations of condition and result: each can be single, multiple collectively (all conditions or results together), or multiple alternatively (one unspecified among several). Multiplying gives nine possibilities. This classification is fundamental for understanding conditional statements in legal texts.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): Something upon which the existence of something else depends, whether by reason (rational shart), divine law (legal shart), or linguistic usage (linguistic shart, meaning a sign).
جزاء (jaza'): The result or consequent that is dependent on the condition; it may be single, multiple collectively, or multiple alternatively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly describes the logical relation between a condition and its result?
A) Existence of condition entails existence of result, and non-existence entails non-existence.
B) Existence of condition entails existence of result, but non-existence does not necessarily entail non-existence.
C) Existence of condition does not necessarily entail existence of result.
D) Non-existence of condition always entails non-existence of result.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, shart is classified into rational, legal, and linguistic, and the logical relation is that existence entails existence, but non-existence does not entail non-existence unless the result is equivalent.

Definition of 'Amr' (Command): Literal and Figurative Meanings
Defines the word 'amr' and discusses its literal meaning, metaphorical use, and linguistic debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the literal and metaphorical meanings of 'amr' and differentiate between the homonym (mushtarak) and univocal (mutawati) interpretations according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The word 'amr' (command) is literally the specific speech demanding an action; its application to the action itself is metaphorical or debated.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' explores the linguistic foundations of 'amr' (command), a key term in usul al-fiqh. Understanding its precise definition is crucial because the legal effect of a command—whether it indicates obligation, recommendation, or permission—depends on its linguistic nature. The passage presents the literal meaning, the possibility of metaphorical usage, and different scholarly views on whether 'amr' is a homonym or univocal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal definition
The passage states that 'amr' is literally (haqiqatan) used for 'al-qawl al-makhsus' – the specific speech that demands an action with necessity (hulman). This is the core meaning.
Note the condition 'hulman' (necessarily) – this distinguishes a command from a mere request or recommendation.
2
Understand the metaphorical usage
The passage mentions that 'amr' can be used metaphorically (majazan) to refer to the action itself (al-fi'l). This usage is recognized by some scholars.
Distinguish between haqiqi (literal) and majazi (figurative) – this is a key distinction in Hanafi linguistic analysis.
3
Recognize the homonym (mushtarak) view
Some scholars argue that 'amr' is a homonym, equally denoting both the speech and the action.
A homonym has multiple unrelated meanings; 'amr' would then have two separate lexical entries.
4
Recognize the univocal (mutawati) view
Others propose that 'amr' is univocal, meaning a common concept that applies to both the speech and the action, e.g., the genus of 'directive'.
A univocal term has a single meaning that applies to multiple individuals, like 'human' to different people.
5
Analyze the objection and refutation
An objection is raised: if 'amr' primarily means the common concept, then using it for the specific speech would be metaphorical. The refutation: applying a general term to a specific instance is not metaphorical – it is a straightforward application.
This refutation relies on the principle that using a general term for a particular is not majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل (في الأمر) * (وهذا اللفظ) أي لفظ الأمر المؤلف من، أ، م، ر (حقيقة في القول المخصوص) وهو قول الطالب للفعل حتماً (مجاز في (الفعل) بكسر الفاء (وقيل) هذا اللفظ (مشترك) بينهما لفظاً (وقيل) هذا اللفظ موضوع (للمشترك بينهما فهو متواطىء (وهو أحدهما) الدائر بين الفعل والقول المخصوص (لا الفعل الأعم من اللساني وغيره (كما قيل لدخول النهي) في الأمر والأخبار وسائر الألفاظ، ولم يقل به أحد، قيل : إنه يلزم على هذا القول كون لفظ الأمر في القول المخصوص مجازاً، ورد بأن استعمال الأعم في الفرد ليس من المجاز في شيء، ولك أن تقول : إن إطلاق الأعم على الأخص بوجهين :
Chapter: On 'Amr' (Command). (* And this word) i.e., the word 'amr' composed of alif, mim, ra (is a literal term for the specific speech) which is the utterance of the requester for an action necessarily (and a metaphorical term for the action) with a kasra on the fa. (And it is said) this word is a homonym between them verbally. (And it is said) this word is placed for the common concept between them, so it is univocal (and it is one of them) i.e., the common concept between the action and the specific speech (not the broader meaning including non-verbal action) as is said due to the inclusion of prohibition in command and reports and other words, and no one has said this. It is said that this opinion necessitates that the word 'amr' in the specific speech is metaphorical, and it is refuted that using the more general for an individual is not metaphorical at all. But you may say: the application of the general to the specific in two ways:
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an usul definition of the term 'amr' (command). The author presents the linguistic analysis of the word, starting with its literal meaning: it is a specific speech that demands an action with necessity. It can be used metaphorically to mean the action itself (with a specific vowelization). There are multiple scholarly opinions: (1) 'amr' is a homonym (mushtarak) between the speech and the action; (2) it is univocal (mutawati) for the common concept between them; (3) it is not broader to include all verbal actions because that would include prohibitions, which no one asserts. An objection is raised that if 'amr' primarily means the general concept (including both speech and action), then using it for the specific speech would be metaphorical. The refutation is that applying a general term to a specific individual is not metaphorical; it is a straightforward usage. The passage ends with a note that the application of the general to the specific can be understood in two ways, but the text cuts off. In Hanafi usul, these distinctions are important because the legal nature of a command (whether it implies obligation, recommendation, etc.) depends on its definition.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): The word composed of alif, mim, ra; it is a literal term for the specific speech that demands an action with necessity.
القول المخصوص (al-qawl al-makhsus): The specific speech that is the command: the utterance of the requester for an action necessarily.
مجاز (majaz): A figurative usage of a word for something other than its original meaning, as when 'amr' is used for the action itself.
مشترك (mushtarak): A word that has multiple meanings and is used for each of them equally; here, some argue 'amr' is a homonym for speech and action.
متواطىء (mutawati): A word that has a single common meaning that applies to multiple individuals, such as a genus; here, some say 'amr' is univocal for the common concept of speech and action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the literal (haqiqi) meaning of the word 'amr'?
A) Any action that is performed.
B) The specific speech that demands an action with necessity.
C) A homonym meaning both command and prohibition.
D) A univocal term for all types of verbal communication.

Key Takeaway
The word 'amr' is literally the specific speech that demands an action necessarily; its application to the action is metaphorical or a matter of scholarly debate.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

8 min
The Hanafi Definition of 'Amr' (Command)
Defining amr as demand of action with superiority and addressing objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'amr' and explain how each component excludes recommendation, supplication, and refraining from action.

Core Idea
Amr is a determinative demand of action from a superior – each term narrows the force of the command.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the precise definition of 'amr' (command) is foundational because it determines which Quranic and prophetic directives impose obligations. This lesson unpacks the standard definition: 'a demand of an action determinatively from a superior.' We will examine how objections from prohibition and 'do not leave' commands are answered, and how the definition was refined to clearly exclude preventing actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four components
The definition consists of iqtiḍā' (demand), fi'l (action), hatman (determinatively), and isti'lā' (superiority). Understand what each component excludes: hatman excludes recommendation (nadb), isti'lā' excludes supplication (du'ā') and request (iltimās), fi'l excludes refraining (kaff) which appears in prohibition.
2
Examine the objection from prohibition (nahy)
Objection: Prohibition seems to demand an action (refraining). How does the definition handle this? The text responds by distinguishing the internal command (al-amr al-nafsi) from the verbal command. The demand of refraining is a command from the perspective of the commander, but a prohibition from the perspective of the commanded. The usuli's focus is on the verbal, so a refined answer is needed.
The refined answer distinguishes primary intention from secondary means.
3
Examine the objection from 'do not leave' (lā tattarik)
The phrase 'do not leave' appears to demand an action (leaving). But the text explains that it demands the omission of the action, not the action itself. The definition's 'fi'l' refers to the root of derivation; 'lā tattarik' does not demand that root. The most appropriate answer is that the primary demand is the initial demand, which is not present in prohibition or 'do not leave' inherently.
This refines the definition to exclude any form of refraining.
4
Note the refined definition including 'ghayr kaff'
Some scholars added 'ghayr kaff' (not refraining) to the definition. This avoids the objection from 'akfif' (restrain). The text explains that the imperative form (sīgha) demands the action, while the lexical root (mādda) may indicate refraining. The definition is about the form, not the root.
Thus 'akfif' is not an 'amr' in the technical sense? No, it is interpreted as a command to perform an action (restraining) which is a positive act, not refraining. The definition stands.

(لا) يشترط (هذا) أي العلو ( ولا ذاك) أي الاستعلاء وبه قال أكثر الشافعية وفي شرح المختصر وهو الحق لقوله تعالى حكاية عن (فرعون إن هَذَا لَسَحْرُ عَلِيمٌ يُرِيدُ أَن يُخْرِجَكُم مِّنْ أَرْضِكُمْ فَمَاذَا وصيرورة عصاه حية وعلم أنه النبي الحق الذي بيده هلاكه وهلاك ملكه (اضطر إلى إعانة العلماء بالتدبيرات التي لا تغني من الحق شيئاً فهناك عنده صحة الاستعلاء بل علو) حقيقة (لأن للعلم درجة في نفس الأمر، وفيه رد على من زعم أنه تام في نفي العلو، ولك أن تقول : إن فرعون إنما سألهم عن أمر يوجب إفحام موسى عليه السلام، ولم يكن شيء في نفس الأمر يوجب إفحامه ويرد الآية العظيمة، بل جل ما كان عندهم من هوساتهم، وكانوا المشاورة) يعني أن المجرد استعمل في معنى المفاعلة (أو مجاز عن الإشارة ابتداء (ورد) بأنه حقيقة في القول بخصوصه فلا يترك الأصل) وفيه أن ههنا ضرورة في ترك الأصل، لأن الاستعلاء معتبر فيه بالدليل الذي لا بقتله فلم يقتله معاوية فأطلقه، ثم أفسد وأراد الخروج، فقال عمرو بن العاص ما قال (وحد القاضي بالقول المقتضي طاعة المأمور بفعل المأمور به وارتضاه جمهور الشافعية وفيه دور من وجهين: أحدهما: المحدود من المأمور والمأمور به في الحد، والآخر من أخذ الطاعة (لأن الطاعة موافقة الأمر، وأجيب أولاً بأن علمنا الأمر من حيث هو كلام كافٍ في علمنا المخاطب به، وهو المأمور و) علمنا (ما يتضمنه الكلام (هو المأمور به وفعل مضمونه وهو طاعته فحينئذ توقف الأمر على هذه الأشياء بوجه، وتوقف هذه الأشياء على معرفته بوجه فلا دور ولا يخفى ما فيه إن أراد من أخذ مشتق
Then [the definition of] amr: a demand of an action determinatively (thereby excluding recommendation) from a superior. An objection was raised against it: (the type 'do not leave out') for its type, which is prohibition, involves a demand of an action—that there is no obligation except in an action, and that is refraining in the chapter of prohibition; and an individual case, for its individual entails the demand of the action whose omission is prohibited. It was answered that what is defined is the internal command (al-amr al-nafsi), so it necessarily involves being a command. For the demand of refraining that exists in the one making the demand is a command with respect to him and a prohibition with respect to the refrained thing. But there is something in it: The usuli's purpose is not concerned with the internal but with words, so it is appropriate to define the verbal command. And it was also answered that by 'action' is meant the root of derivation; prohibition does not involve demanding the root action but rather another action which is refraining. Similarly, 'do not leave out' in particular does not demand the leaving out which is the root of derivation. The most appropriate answer is that the primary intention is the initial demand, and that is not present in prohibition inherently because the purpose in prohibition is the non-existence of the prohibited action. But since it is not within the slave's capacity to demand the refraining which is the means, its demand was not a primary intention. In 'do not leave out', the absence of demand for the action whose omission is prohibited is more apparent primarily and inherently. However, there remains here that then it does not apply to 'restrain' (akfif). How? There is no difference between 'restrain from zina' and 'do not approach zina' in that the primary purpose is the absence of zina which is the commanded thing; the command to restrain is only a means to it. Claiming that the restraint in the first is primary and not in the second is arbitrary. So reflect and be fair. Then some added and said: 'the demand of an action, not refraining, determinatively, etc.' In that case, the question does not arise from the beginning, but it is still open to refutation with examples like 'restrain' (akfif). And it was answered that the intended meaning is the demand of action with respect to the form and structure; examples like 'restrain' indicate the demand of refraining by the lexical root, but the form is a demand and request for action. So understand. As for superiority, as a precaution against supplication and request, it is a condition in command according to most of our scholars among the Maturidi masters and al-Amidi from the Ash'aris, and it was declared correct in al-Mahsul by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who is also Ash'ari; and it is the view of Abu al-Husayn from the Mu'tazilites. [Evidence:] Reasonable people blame a lower person for commanding a superior. That is, if a lower person says to a superior 'I command you to do such-and-such,' they blame him. If highness were a condition, they would not have this statement, let alone blame. And if superiority were not a condition, blame would not be justified, just as if he said 'I called upon you for such-and-such.' So understand, and do not pay attention to those who claim that this evidence does not negate highness; there is no approximation. According to the Mu'tazila, highness is necessary in command; otherwise it would be a supplication or request, and they have no evidence for it. According to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, neither this (i.e., highness) nor that (i.e., superiority) is a condition; and this is the view of most Shafi'is, and in Sharh al-Mukhtar it is the truth, due to the Almighty's words relating from Pharaoh: 'Indeed this is a great sorcery; he wants to expel you from your land. So what do you command?' (al-A'raf 109-110). The people had no highness in his view nor superiority, for they were in their foolishness thinking him a lord. There is in it that when Pharaoh was seized by fear when he saw the clear sign and manifest miracle of the white hand and his staff becoming a serpent, and he knew that he was the true prophet by whose hand his own destruction and the destruction of his kingdom would occur, he was forced to seek the help of the [so-called] scholars with plans that were of no avail against the truth. So there was, in his view, the validity of superiority, indeed highness in reality, because knowledge has a degree in itself. In this is a refutation of those who claimed it is perfect in negating highness. But you may say: Pharaoh only asked them for something that would refute Moses, peace be upon him, and there was nothing that would in fact refute him and repel the great sign. Rather, what they had were merely their delusions; they had fallen into compounded ignorance in what they claimed to be refuting and silencing. The ignorant obstinate person is low in reality, and there is no aspect of highness at all. In short, their superiority was due to Pharaoh thinking them scholars, and they thinking themselves so. So understand. And it was said that it is a metaphor from al-mu'amarah (consultation), meaning the bare form is used in the sense of mutual participation, or a metaphor for initiation. It was rejected because it is literal for the specific speech, so the original should not be abandoned. There is in it that here there is a necessity to abandon the original because superiority is considered in it by evidence that cannot be refuted. Understand. And similar to it is his statement, i.e., the saying of Amr ibn al-As to Muawiya, or Husayn ibn al-Mundhir to Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, as in al-Taqrir and al-Hashiya: 'I commanded you with a decisive command and you disobeyed me, so you became stripped of authority, regretful.' For Amr ibn al-As had no superiority over Muawiya because he was among his followers, nor did Husayn have over Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, yet they used the word 'amr'. The story in the first interpretation is that Ibn Hashim rebelled repeatedly against Muawiya and was captured; Amr ibn al-As once advised executing him but Muawiya did not, so he released him. Then he corrupted [again] and wanted to go out, so Amr ibn al-As said what he said. And al-Qadi defined amr as 'a statement that necessitates the obedience of the commanded person to perform the commanded act,' and the majority of Shafi'is approved it. In it there is circularity from two aspects: one: the defined includes 'commanded' and 'commanded act' in the definition; the other: taking 'obedience' (since obedience is compliance with the command). It was answered firstly that our knowledge of the command as speech is sufficient for our knowledge of the addressee, who is the commanded person, and our knowledge of what the speech contains (that is the commanded act and the performance of its content, which is obedience). Then the command depends on these things in one respect, and these things depend on knowing it in another respect, so there is no circularity. But what it contains is not hidden if he intended to take a derivative.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is primarily a definition of the term 'amr' (command) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, followed by objections and responses, and a detailed discussion on whether superiority (istil'aa) or highness (uluww) is a necessary condition for a command. The text begins by stating the definition: 'amr is a demand of an action determinatively from a superior' (iqtiḍā' fi'l hatman isti'lā'). The definition excludes recommendation (nadb) and includes the condition of superiority to exclude supplication (du'ā') and request (iltimās). Several objections are raised: the type 'do not leave' (lā tattarik) and prohibition (nahy) seem to involve a demand of action, but the author answers that the definition deals with the internal command (al-amr al-nafsi) and that the demand in prohibition is for refraining, not for the action itself. A more refined answer distinguishes the initial demand from secondary means. The definition is also refined by some to include 'a demand of an action, not refraining'. The second major topic is the condition of superiority itself. The text reports three positions: Maturidi and some Ash'aris (like al-Amidi and al-Razi) hold that superiority is a necessary condition; Mu'tazilites require highness (uluww) in the commander; Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and most Shafi'is hold that neither superiority nor highness is a condition, as evidenced by the Quranic verse where Pharaoh asks his people 'what do you command?' despite their inferiority. The author offers a detailed analysis of the Pharaoh story, arguing that in reality they had no superiority but were perceived as scholars. An additional argument from the story of Amr ibn al-As addressing Muawiya shows that the word 'amr' can be used without actual superiority. Finally, the text mentions al-Qadi's definition (al-Qadi is likely al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar or another) and addresses the circularity objection in his definition.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A demand of an action determinatively from a superior (اقتضاء فعل حتماً استعلاء).
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā'): The act of demanding; in the definition of 'amr' it is the demand inherent in the command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which component of the Hanafi definition of 'amr' excludes recommendation (nadb)?
A) iqtiḍā'
B) fi'l
C) hatman
D) isti'lā'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'amr' as a determinative demand from a superior is carefully crafted to exclude recommendation, prohibition, and requests, ensuring only binding commands are included.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced

The Condition of Superiority in 'Amr': A Madhhab Dispute
Dispute over the condition of superiority in the definition of 'amr'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the Hanafi (Maturidi) position on the necessity of superiority in 'amr', and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the example of Pharaoh.

Core Idea
The condition of superiority in 'amr' is defended by Hanafis with rational evidence and reconciled with apparent Quranic counter-examples.

One of the most debated issues in the definition of 'amr' is whether the commander must be in a position of superiority (istilā') or at least of highness (uluww). This lesson explores the positions of the Maturidi Hanafis, Mu'tazilites, and Ash'aris/Shafi'is. We will analyze the key evidence: the blame argument from reason and the Quranic verse of Pharaoh, and see how each school handles the apparent counter-example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
The text presents three positions: (1) Maturidi Hanafis and some Ash'aris require superiority (istilā'). (2) Mu'tazilites require highness (uluww). (3) Ash'ari and most Shafi'is require neither.
2
Examine the rational evidence for istilā'
The blame argument: Reasonable people blame a lower person for commanding a higher (يذم العقلاء الأدنى بأمر الأعلى). If superiority were not a condition, this blame would not make sense. This is the primary Hanafi proof.
The text notes that this evidence does not negate highness but establishes superiority.
3
Examine the Quranic counter-evidence
The verse where Pharaoh asks his people 'فَمَاذَا تَأْمُرُونَ' (So what do you command?) seems to show that the word 'amr' was used without superiority, as Pharaoh was higher in rank. The Shafi'is use this to argue that superiority is not a condition.
4
Analyze the Hanafi responses
The text offers several responses: (a) Pharaoh's question is metaphorical, meaning consultation (al-mu'amarah). (b) The people had a relative superiority due to Pharaoh's fear and their perceived knowledge. (c) The literal meaning is abandoned due to the strong rational proof for superiority.
Also the story of Amr ibn al-As commanding Muawiya is addressed similarly.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a lower person uses the imperative to a higher, is it a valid 'amr'? Hanafis say no; it is a supplication or request. Shafi'is say it is still an 'amr' by form, though obedience may not be required due to lack of authority.
This distinction affects how we interpret non-binding commands in sacred texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بأن المحدود) الأمر (النفسي فيلتزم) كونه أمراً، فإن طلب الكف القائم بالطالب أمر بالنسبة إليه ونهي بالنسبة إلى المكفوف عنه وفيه ما فيه) فإن غرض الأصولي لم يتعلق بالنفسي، بل بالألفاظ، فالمناسب أن يحد الأمر اللفظي (و) أجيب (بأن المراد بالفعل (فعل) هو مبدأ (الاشتقاق فالنهي ليس فيه طلب الفعل المبدأ بل لفعل آخر هو الكف، وكذا لا تترك بخصوصه، فإنه لم يطلب فيه الترك الذي هو مبدأ الاشتقاق (والأوجه) في الجواب أن المتبادر الاقتضاء الأولي، وذلك ليس في النهي بالذات، لأن المقصود في النهي عدم وجود الفعل المنهي لكن لما لم يكن في وسع العبد طلب الكف الذي هو الوسيلة فلم يكن اقتضاؤه مقصوداً أولياً، وفي لا وإنما أمر : بالكف لكونه وسيلة إليه، ودعوى كون الكف في الأول مقصوداً بالذات دون الثاني تحكم فتدبر ،وأنصف ثم إن البعض زادوا وقالوا : اقتضاء فعل غير كف حتماً إلى آخره، فحينئذ لا يتوجه إليه السؤال من الأصل، لكن يتوجه إليه النقض وبنحو أكفف، وأجيب بأن المراد اقتضاء الفعل بالنظر إلى الهيئة والصيغة، ونحو اكفف إنما يدل على اقتضاء الكف بالمادة، والهيئة إنما هي اقتضاء وطلب للفعل فافهم (وأما) الاستعلاء احترازاً عن الدعاء والالتماس فهو شرط) في الأمر (عند أكثر أصحابنا من المشايخ الماتريدية والآمدي ) من الأشعري (وصححه في المحصول) للإمام فخر الدين الرازي من الأشعرية أيضاً وهو رأي أبي الحسين) من المعتزلة الذم العقلاء الأدنى بأمر الأعلى) يعني : لو قال الأدنى للأعلى: أمرتك بكذا يذمونه، فلو كان العلو معتبراً لما هذا القول فضلاً عن الذم ، ولو لم يكن الاستعلاء معتبراً لما صح توجه الذم كما إذا قال : دعوت منك كذا ،فافهم ولا تلتفت إلى قول من قال : إن هذا الوجه لا يدل على نفي العلو فلا تقريب ( وعند المعتزلة يجب العلو) في الأمر (وإلا كان دعاء أو التماساً وليس لهم دليل عليه (وعند) الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري (لا) يشترط (هذا) أي العلو ( ولا ذاك) أي الاستعلاء وبه قال أكثر الشافعية وفي شرح المختصر وهو الحق لقوله تعالى حكاية عن (فرعون إن هَذَا لَسَحْرُ عَلِيمٌ يُرِيدُ أَن يُخْرِجَكُم مِّنْ أَرْضِكُمْ فَمَاذَا تَأْمُرُونَ ) [الأعراف : ۱۰۹ - ۱۱۰] ولم يكن للقوم علو عنده ولا استعلاء فإنهم كانوا في حماقتهم من ظنهم إياه رباً وفيه أن فرعون لما أخذته (الدهشة لما رأى من الآية البيئة والمعجزة الظاهرة من اليد البيضاء وصيرورة عصاه حية وعلم أنه النبي الحق الذي بيده هلاكه وهلاك ملكه (اضطر إلى إعانة العلماء بالتدبيرات التي لا تغني من الحق شيئاً فهناك عنده صحة الاستعلاء بل علو) حقيقة (لأن للعلم درجة في نفس الأمر، وفيه رد على من زعم أنه تام في نفي العلو، ولك أن تقول : إن فرعون إنما سألهم عن أمر يوجب إفحام موسى عليه السلام، ولم يكن شيء في نفس الأمر يوجب إفحامه ويرد الآية العظيمة، بل جل ما كان عندهم من هوساتهم، وكانوا قد وقعوا في الجهل المركب فيما زعموه مفحماً مسكتاً وللجاهل المكابر دنو في نفس الأمر ولا وجه للعلو أصلاً، وقصارى الأمر استعلاؤهم لظن فرعون إياهم علماء، وظنهم أنفسهم كذلك فافهم (وقيل) مجاز من المؤامرة أي المشاورة) يعني أن المجرد استعمل في معنى المفاعلة (أو مجاز عن الإشارة ابتداء (ورد) بأنه حقيقة في القول بخصوصه فلا يترك الأصل) وفيه أن ههنا ضرورة في ترك الأصل، لأن الاستعلاء معتبر فيه بالدليل الذي لا مرد له فافهم ونظيره) (قوله أي قول عمرو بن العاص لمعاوية، أو حصين بن المنذر ليزيد بن المهلب كذا في التقرير، كذا في «الحاشية» : أمرتك أمراً جازماً فــعــصــيــتــنــي فأصبحت مسلوب الإمارة نادما فإنه لم يكن لعمرو بن العاص استعلاء على معاوية لأنه من متبعيه، ولا لحصين على يزيد بن المهلب، مع أنهما استعملا لفظ الأمر، والقصة على التقدير الأول أن ابن هاشم خرج مراراً على معاوية فأسر، فأشار عمرو بن العاص مرة بقتله فلم يقتله معاوية فأطلقه، ثم أفسد وأراد الخروج، فقال عمرو بن العاص ما قال (وحد القاضي بالقول المقتضي طاعة المأمور بفعل المأمور به وارتضاه جمهور الشافعية وفيه دور من وجهين: أحدهما: المحدود من المأمور والمأمور به في الحد، والآخر من أخذ الطاعة (لأن الطاعة موافقة الأمر، وأجيب أولاً بأن علمنا الأمر من حيث هو كلام كافٍ في علمنا المخاطب به، وهو المأمور و) علمنا (ما يتضمنه الكلام (هو المأمور به وفعل مضمونه وهو طاعته فحينئذ توقف الأمر على هذه الأشياء بوجه، وتوقف هذه الأشياء على معرفته بوجه فلا دور ولا يخفى ما فيه إن أراد من أخذ مشتق
Then [the definition of] amr: a demand of an action determinatively (thereby excluding recommendation) from a superior. An objection was raised against it: (the type 'do not leave out') for its type, which is prohibition, involves a demand of an action—that there is no obligation except in an action, and that is refraining in the chapter of prohibition; and an individual case, for its individual entails the demand of the action whose omission is prohibited. It was answered that what is defined is the internal command (al-amr al-nafsi), so it necessarily involves being a command. For the demand of refraining that exists in the one making the demand is a command with respect to him and a prohibition with respect to the refrained thing. But there is something in it: The usuli's purpose is not concerned with the internal but with words, so it is appropriate to define the verbal command. And it was also answered that by 'action' is meant the root of derivation; prohibition does not involve demanding the root action but rather another action which is refraining. Similarly, 'do not leave out' in particular does not demand the leaving out which is the root of derivation. The most appropriate answer is that the primary intention is the initial demand, and that is not present in prohibition inherently because the purpose in prohibition is the non-existence of the prohibited action. But since it is not within the slave's capacity to demand the refraining which is the means, its demand was not a primary intention. In 'do not leave out', the absence of demand for the action whose omission is prohibited is more apparent primarily and inherently. However, there remains here that then it does not apply to 'restrain' (akfif). How? There is no difference between 'restrain from zina' and 'do not approach zina' in that the primary purpose is the absence of zina which is the commanded thing; the command to restrain is only a means to it. Claiming that the restraint in the first is primary and not in the second is arbitrary. So reflect and be fair. Then some added and said: 'the demand of an action, not refraining, determinatively, etc.' In that case, the question does not arise from the beginning, but it is still open to refutation with examples like 'restrain' (akfif). And it was answered that the intended meaning is the demand of action with respect to the form and structure; examples like 'restrain' indicate the demand of refraining by the lexical root, but the form is a demand and request for action. So understand. As for superiority, as a precaution against supplication and request, it is a condition in command according to most of our scholars among the Maturidi masters and al-Amidi from the Ash'aris, and it was declared correct in al-Mahsul by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who is also Ash'ari; and it is the view of Abu al-Husayn from the Mu'tazilites. [Evidence:] Reasonable people blame a lower person for commanding a superior. That is, if a lower person says to a superior 'I command you to do such-and-such,' they blame him. If highness were a condition, they would not have this statement, let alone blame. And if superiority were not a condition, blame would not be justified, just as if he said 'I called upon you for such-and-such.' So understand, and do not pay attention to those who claim that this evidence does not negate highness; there is no approximation. According to the Mu'tazila, highness is necessary in command; otherwise it would be a supplication or request, and they have no evidence for it. According to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, neither this (i.e., highness) nor that (i.e., superiority) is a condition; and this is the view of most Shafi'is, and in Sharh al-Mukhtar it is the truth, due to the Almighty's words relating from Pharaoh: 'Indeed this is a great sorcery; he wants to expel you from your land. So what do you command?' (al-A'raf 109-110). The people had no highness in his view nor superiority, for they were in their foolishness thinking him a lord. There is in it that when Pharaoh was seized by fear when he saw the clear sign and manifest miracle of the white hand and his staff becoming a serpent, and he knew that he was the true prophet by whose hand his own destruction and the destruction of his kingdom would occur, he was forced to seek the help of the [so-called] scholars with plans that were of no avail against the truth. So there was, in his view, the validity of superiority, indeed highness in reality, because knowledge has a degree in itself. In this is a refutation of those who claimed it is perfect in negating highness. But you may say: Pharaoh only asked them for something that would refute Moses, peace be upon him, and there was nothing that would in fact refute him and repel the great sign. Rather, what they had were merely their delusions; they had fallen into compounded ignorance in what they claimed to be refuting and silencing. The ignorant obstinate person is low in reality, and there is no aspect of highness at all. In short, their superiority was due to Pharaoh thinking them scholars, and they thinking themselves so. So understand. And it was said that it is a metaphor from al-mu'amarah (consultation), meaning the bare form is used in the sense of mutual participation, or a metaphor for initiation. It was rejected because it is literal for the specific speech, so the original should not be abandoned. There is in it that here there is a necessity to abandon the original because superiority is considered in it by evidence that cannot be refuted. Understand. And similar to it is his statement, i.e., the saying of Amr ibn al-As to Muawiya, or Husayn ibn al-Mundhir to Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, as in al-Taqrir and al-Hashiya: 'I commanded you with a decisive command and you disobeyed me, so you became stripped of authority, regretful.' For Amr ibn al-As had no superiority over Muawiya because he was among his followers, nor did Husayn have over Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, yet they used the word 'amr'. The story in the first interpretation is that Ibn Hashim rebelled repeatedly against Muawiya and was captured; Amr ibn al-As once advised executing him but Muawiya did not, so he released him. Then he corrupted [again] and wanted to go out, so Amr ibn al-As said what he said. And al-Qadi defined amr as 'a statement that necessitates the obedience of the commanded person to perform the commanded act,' and the majority of Shafi'is approved it. In it there is circularity from two aspects: one: the defined includes 'commanded' and 'commanded act' in the definition; the other: taking 'obedience' (since obedience is compliance with the command). It was answered firstly that our knowledge of the command as speech is sufficient for our knowledge of the addressee, who is the commanded person, and our knowledge of what the speech contains (that is the commanded act and the performance of its content, which is obedience). Then the command depends on these things in one respect, and these things depend on knowing it in another respect, so there is no circularity. But what it contains is not hidden if he intended to take a derivative.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is primarily a definition of the term 'amr' (command) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, followed by objections and responses, and a detailed discussion on whether superiority (istil'aa) or highness (uluww) is a necessary condition for a command. The text begins by stating the definition: 'amr is a demand of an action determinatively from a superior' (iqtiḍā' fi'l hatman isti'lā'). The definition excludes recommendation (nadb) and includes the condition of superiority to exclude supplication (du'ā') and request (iltimās). Several objections are raised: the type 'do not leave' (lā tattarik) and prohibition (nahy) seem to involve a demand of action, but the author answers that the definition deals with the internal command (al-amr al-nafsi) and that the demand in prohibition is for refraining, not for the action itself. A more refined answer distinguishes the initial demand from secondary means. The definition is also refined by some to include 'a demand of an action, not refraining'. The second major topic is the condition of superiority itself. The text reports three positions: Maturidi and some Ash'aris (like al-Amidi and al-Razi) hold that superiority is a necessary condition; Mu'tazilites require highness (uluww) in the commander; Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and most Shafi'is hold that neither superiority nor highness is a condition, as evidenced by the Quranic verse where Pharaoh asks his people 'what do you command?' despite their inferiority. The author offers a detailed analysis of the Pharaoh story, arguing that in reality they had no superiority but were perceived as scholars. An additional argument from the story of Amr ibn al-As addressing Muawiya shows that the word 'amr' can be used without actual superiority. Finally, the text mentions al-Qadi's definition (al-Qadi is likely al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar or another) and addresses the circularity objection in his definition.

Key Terms
الاستعلاء (al-isti'lā'): Superiority or being in a higher position; a condition for the validity of a command according to the Maturidi Hanafis.
العلو (al-uluww): Highness; the condition required by the Mu'tazilites for a valid command.
المؤامرة (al-mu'āmarah): Consultation; a metaphorical interpretation of the Pharaoh verse offered by Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi Hanafis, what is the condition for a valid 'amr'?
A) Highness (uluww)
B) Superiority (isti'lā')
C) Knowledge ('ilm)
D) Fear (khawf)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi condition of superiority in 'amr' preserves the distinction between genuine command and mere request, grounded in rational blame and reconciled with Quranic examples through contextual interpretation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
Defining Amr: The Command and Its Conditions
Defining amr and debating the necessity of 'uluww and isti'la'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of amr, identify the disputed condition of superiority, and apply the Quranic proof to refute the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
A command does not require actual superiority; seeking superiority (isti'la') in speech suffices.

In Usul al-Fiqh, defining 'amr (command) is foundational because commands trigger obligations. The Hanafi school, aligned with the Ash'aris, holds that a command is valid even if the commander is inferior, as long as the utterance conveys seeking superiority. This lesson unpacks the definition of amr, the dispute with the Mu'tazila, and the logical challenges of defining such a relational concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the two main definitions: the Mu'tazila view (command = saying 'do' to one below) and al-Qadi's definition (a saying requiring obedience by performing the commanded act). Note how each definition builds in conditions.
Al-Qadi's definition is more technical and introduces the problem of circularity.
2
Examine the superiority dispute
Compare the positions: Mu'tazila require actual superiority (uluww); Ash'ari/Hanafi require only seeking superiority (isti'la') or neither. Identify the proof from Quran 7:109-110 and the poetic analogy of Amr ibn al-As.
Watch the counter-argument about Pharaoh's state; the author refutes it.
3
Analyze the circularity (dawr) in al-Qadi's definition
The definition includes 'the commanded person' and 'obedience', which presuppose knowledge of the command. Three responses are given to break the circularity: knowledge via the aspect of speech, knowledge via accidental aspects, and presential vs. acquired knowledge.
Each response is critiqued; the third is particularly weak because definition requires conceptualization.
4
Apply the Hanafi stance on amr
From the preferred view, derive that a command is a speech act requiring decisive obedience, with isti'la' as sufficient. Contrast with the Mu'tazila condition of superiority and with threats or reports.
The distinction between direct commands and reported commands (e.g., 'amartuka') is crucial.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a slave commanding his master with isti'la' is linguistically a command (though not legally binding per se). The Mu'tazila would deny it is a command at all.
This shows how the definition affects the scope of obligation.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
۳۹۳
مطلق الكلام فلا يكفي في معرفة حقيقة المأمور به وفهم الطاعة ،بعد ، وإن أريد الكلام المقيد بالقيود فالدور عائد قطعاً، كذا في «التحرير»، وهذا غير وافٍ فإن مراد المجيب أن الأمر معلوم بوجه عرضي وهو الكلام، والمقصود معرفته بالكنه أو بالرسم الجامع المانع، ومعرفته بهذا الوجه تستلزم معرفة المأمور بوجه كونه هو المخاطب بالكلام وكذا المأمور به بوجه كونه مضمون الكلام، وكذا الطاعة بوجه كونه إتياناً لمضمونه، فأخذت هذه الأشياء المعلومة بالأوجه المذكورة في الحد أو الرسم وخرج الحاصل أن الأمر الكلام المقتضي إتيان المخاطب بمضمون ما خوطب به فلا إيراد أصلاً لكن ينبغي أن يراد بالاقتضاء الاقتضاء الحتمي إلا أن يبنى على ما اشتهر عن الشافعية أن المندوب مأمور به ، فلا إشكال بوجه فتدبر فيه (و) أجيب (ثانياً بأن معرفته بوجه عرضي يمتاز به عن جميع ما عداه حاصل لكل عارف باللغة قبل التحديد والمقصود معرفة حده فحينئذ يحصل معرفة هذه الأشياء الموقوفة على الأمر، فتؤخذ في الحد فيحصل به حقيقة الأمر، فلا دور لتغاير الموقوف والموقوف عليه، والفرق بين هذا والأول بعد الاشتراك في إحداث التغاير بين الموقوف والموقوف عليه أن الموقوف عليه هناك الأمر المعلوم بالوجه العام وههنا المعلوم بوجه جامع مانع (أقول : إن قلت : أخذ العرضي في الحد يخرجه عن الحدية لأن المجموع من الداخل والخارج (خارج) ولا يصح الحدية، وههنا قد أخذ المأمور والمأمور به وكلاهما خارجان عن حقيقة الأمر، قلت: قد تكون (الحقيقة المحدودة ذات تعلق وإضافة لا يمكن ملاحظتها إلا بلحاظ المتعلقات (فملاحظة المتعلقات لتحصيل تلك الحقيقة لا يخرجه عن الحدية) كما في حد أحد المتضايفين، فإنه لا بد فيه من أخذ المضايف الآخر فإنه لا يلزم أن يعتبر) الخارج ( محمولاً) بل إنما يعتبره متعلقاً من المتعلقات فافهم (و) أجيب (ثالثاً بأن حصول الشيء بنفسه) في العلم الحضوري (غير حصوله بصورته الذي هو التصور المطلوب في العلم الحصولي، فالمطلوب العلم بالمحدود الحصولي، والموقوف عليه بهذه الأشياء الحضوري فلا دور لاختلاف نحو الإدراك (ورد) هذا الجواب بأن ما أخذ في التعريف لا بد من تصوره) ولا يكفي حضوره (لأنه) أي التعريف (تحصيل صورة من صور متعددة فلم يختلف جهة الإدراك أصلاً (ولهذا لم يكن الحضوري كاسباً إذ لا حصول فيه أصلاً فتفكر، ثم هو منقوض بأمرتك بفعل كذا) لأنه قول يقتضي طاعة المأمور بفعل المأمور به إلا أن يخص الأمر (النفسي بالتعريف، ويلتزم كونه أمراً) واعلم أن هذا الإيراد غير مختص بتعريف القاضي، بل وارد على المختار أيضاً، فالجواب أنه خبر عن الأمر وليس فيه اقتضاء لفعل بل إخبار عنه، وكذا ليس قولاً مقتضياً لطاعة المأمور بالذات بل إخبار عن قول كذلك، ومن ههنا ظهر سقوط التزام كونه أمراً باعتبار النفسي فإنه إخبار عن أمر فإن أريد به إنشاء الأمر مجازاً فهو أمر لفظياً كان أو نفسياً، فإنه لا فرق بينه وبين افعل كذا فتدبر (و) قال (جمهور المعتزلة) الأمر (قول القائل لمن دونه افعل وأورد عليه (التهديد ونحوه) فإنه قوله لمن دونه افعل مع أنه ليس أمراً (و) أورد (قول الحاكي والمبلغ، وأمر الأدنى للأعلى (استعلاء) فإنها أوامر مع عدم صدق الحد عليها، لأن ما عدا الأخير ليس
In al-Mahsul) by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi from the Ash'aris, and it is also the view of Abu al-Husayn from the Mu'tazila: 'The intellect censures the inferior for commanding the superior.' Meaning: if the inferior says to the superior 'I commanded you to do such-and-such', they censure him. So if superiority were a condition, there would be no such statement, let alone censure. And if seeking superiority were not a condition, the censure would not be valid, as when he says 'I requested such-and-such from you'. So understand, and do not pay attention to one who says this argument does not negate superiority, so do not approximate. (And according to the Mu'tazila, superiority is obligatory) in command (otherwise it would be a supplication or request, and they have no proof for it. (And according to) Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (this) i.e. superiority (is not required, nor that) i.e. seeking superiority, and most Shafi'is hold this view. In Sharh al-Mukhtasar: 'And it is the truth, because of the Almighty's statement narrating about Pharaoh: “Indeed this is a great sorcery; he wants to drive you out of your land, so what do you command?” [al-A'raf: 109-110]. And the people had no superiority over him nor seeking superiority, for they were in their foolishness thinking him a lord.' And in it: When Pharaoh was struck with confusion by the clear sign and manifest miracle of the white hand and his staff becoming a serpent, and he knew that he was the true prophet by whose hand his destruction and the destruction of his kingdom would occur, he was forced to seek help from the scholars with schemes that avail nothing against the truth. So there was from him the validity of seeking superiority, rather actual superiority (because knowledge has a station in reality.) And in it is a refutation of one who claims that it is complete in negating superiority. But you may say: Pharaoh only asked them about a matter that would necessarily refute Musa, peace be upon him, and there was nothing in reality that would refute him and repel the great sign. Rather, the most they had was their delusions, and they had fallen into compound ignorance regarding what they claimed was a refuting and silencing argument. And the stubborn ignorant one has lowliness in reality, and there is no aspect of superiority at all. In short, their seeking superiority was due to Pharaoh's assumption that they were scholars, and their own assumption of that. So understand. (And it was said) it is a metonymy from 'mu'amarah' meaning consultation, meaning that the simple form is used in the sense of the mutual form. (Or a metonymy for indicating initially. (And it was refuted) because it is a reality in speech particular to it, so the original is not abandoned. And in it: there is a necessity here to abandon the original, because seeking superiority is considered in it by a proof that cannot be denied, so understand. And its analogy) is his statement, i.e. the statement of Amr ibn al-As to Mu'awiya, or Husayn ibn al-Mundhir to Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, as in al-Taqrir, as in al-Hashiya: 'I commanded you with a decisive command, so you disobeyed me, so you became bereft of authority, regretful.' For Amr ibn al-As had no seeking superiority over Mu'awiya because he was among his followers, nor did Husayn over Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, yet they both used the word 'command'. The story according to the first estimation: Ibn Hashim had rebelled against Mu'awiya repeatedly and was captured. Amr ibn al-As once advised killing him, but Mu'awiya did not kill him and released him. Then he caused corruption and wanted to rebel, so Amr ibn al-As said what he said. (And al-Qadi defined it as: 'A saying requiring the obedience of the commanded person by performing the commanded act.' And the majority of Shafi'is approved it. But there is circularity in two respects: one is that the defined includes the commanded person and the commanded act in the definition, and the other is taking 'obedience' (because obedience is compliance with the command). And it was answered firstly: that our knowledge of the command insofar as it is speech is sufficient for our knowledge of the addressee, i.e. the commanded person, and our knowledge of what the speech contains (is the commanded act), and performing its content is his obedience. So then the command is dependent on these things in one respect, and these things are dependent on knowledge of it in one respect, so there is no circularity. But it is not hidden what is in it if he meant absolute speech, for it does not suffice for knowledge of the reality of the commanded act and understanding obedience afterwards. And if he meant speech qualified by conditions, then circularity returns absolutely. This is in al-Tahrir. And this is insufficient, for the intention of the respondent is that the command is known from one accidental aspect, which is speech, and the goal is to know it by essence or by a comprehensive and excluding definition. And knowing it from this aspect entails knowledge of the commanded person from the aspect of being the addressee of the speech, and similarly the commanded act from the aspect of being the content of the speech, and similarly obedience from the aspect of performing its content. So these things known by the mentioned aspects were taken in the definition or description, and the result is that the command is: 'the speech requiring the addressee to perform the content of what he was addressed with.' So there is no objection at all. But 'requiring' should mean decisive requiring, unless based on what is famous from the Shafi'is that the recommended is commanded, so there is no issue from one aspect. So ponder over it. (And it was answered secondly: that knowledge of it from an accidental aspect that distinguishes it from all else is obtained by every linguist before defining, and the goal is to know its definition. So then knowledge of these things that are dependent on the command is obtained, so they are taken in the definition, and by it the reality of command is obtained, so there is no circularity due to the difference between the dependent and that on which it depends. And the difference between this and the first after sharing in bringing about the difference between the dependent and that on which it depends is that that on which it depends there is the command known by the general aspect, and here it is known by a comprehensive and preventing aspect. (I say: if you say: taking the accidental in the definition removes it from being a definition because the whole from the internal and external is external, and definition is not valid. And here the commanded person and commanded act are taken, and both are external to the reality of command. I say: sometimes the defined reality has a relation and addition that cannot be observed except by observing the related things. Observing the related things to obtain that reality does not remove it from being a definition, just as in the definition of one of the two correlatives, for it is necessary to take the other correlative. For it is not necessary that the external be considered a predicate, but rather it is considered a related thing from among the related things. So understand. (And it was answered thirdly: that the attainment of the thing by itself) in presential knowledge (is different from its attainment by its form, which is the conceptualization sought in acquired knowledge. So what is sought is acquired knowledge of the defined, and that on which it depends by these things is presential knowledge, so there is no circularity due to the difference in the mode of perception. (This answer was refuted) because what is taken in the definition must be conceptualized, and presential presence does not suffice (because it) i.e. the definition (is the production of a form among multiple forms, so the mode of perception did not differ at all). (And for this reason presential knowledge is not acquisitive, as there is no attainment in it at all. So reflect. Then it is invalidated by 'I commanded you to do such-and-such' because it is a saying that requires the obedience of the commanded person by performing the commanded act, unless one specifies the definition to the internal command and commits to it being a command. And know that this objection is not specific to al-Qadi's definition, but applies to the preferred view as well. The answer is that it is a report about the command and does not contain requiring an action, but rather reports about it. Similarly, it is not a saying that essentially requires the obedience of the commanded person, but rather a report about such a saying. From here the collapse of the commitment to it being a command from the perspective of the internal command becomes apparent, for it is a report about a command. If by it the creation of the command metaphorically is intended, then it is a command, whether verbal or internal, for there is no difference between it and 'do such-and-such'. So ponder. (And the majority of the Mu'tazila said) (command is: 'the saying of a speaker to one below him: do.' And it was objected to by (threat and the like) for it is his saying to one below him 'do' while it is not a command. Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage treats the definition of 'amr (command) in usul al-fiqh, specifically the conditions of 'uluww (superiority) and isti'la' (seeking superiority). The author presents multiple positions:

- The Ash'ari position (supported by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) holds that neither actual superiority nor seeking superiority is required for a command. Evidence is drawn from Quran 7:109-110 where Pharaoh asks his advisors 'what do you command?'—Pharaoh's people had no superiority over him, yet the word 'amr' is used. The author also discusses a counter-argument about Pharaoh's state and the reality of their ignorance.
- The Mu'tazila position requires actual superiority; without it, the utterance would be a supplication or request.
- The author refines the Ash'ari view and then transitions to al-Qadi's definition: 'a saying requiring the obedience of the commanded person by performing the commanded act.' This definition is critiqued for circularity (al-dawr): it includes the commanded person and act, and obedience, which presuppose knowledge of the command. Three responses are given:
  1. Knowledge of the command from its aspect as speech suffices; the parts are known in different respects, eliminating circularity.
  2. The accidental aspect (speech) is known prior to definition, so the dependent parts can be taken in the definition without circularity.
  3. Presential knowledge (al-'ilm al-huduri) of the command differs from the acquired conceptualization (al-'ilm al-husuli) sought in definition, so no circularity.
- The responses are themselves critiqued, especially the third, because definition requires conceptualization, not mere presence.
- An objection is raised regarding expressions like 'I commanded you' (amartuka) which are reports, not commands. The answer distinguishes between direct commands (e.g., 'do') and reports of commands.
- The Mu'tazila definition (command = 'saying do to one below') is objected to by cases of threats, narration, and a subordinate command to a superior.

Throughout, the commentary serves to sharpen the Hanafi understanding of 'amr', emphasizing that it is a speech act requiring obedience (with decisive requiring, not mere recommendation) without necessitating actual hierarchical superiority.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The speech that requires the commanded person to perform the commanded act (القول المقتضي طاعة المأمور بفعل المأمور به).
الاستعلاء (al-isti'la'): Seeking superiority; the intent of the speaker to show his higher rank even if not actually superior.
الدور (al-dawr): Circularity in definition where the definiendum and definiens depend on each other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, which of the following is required for an utterance to be considered a command (amr)?
A) Actual superiority of the commander over the commanded
B) Seeking superiority (isti'la') in the utterance
C) That the commander be a free adult male
D) That the commanded person be capable of obedience

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'amr as a speech act requiring obedience, without the condition of actual superiority; seeking superiority in the utterance is sufficient, as proven by Quranic and poetic evidence.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
12 min
The Imperative Form for Recommendation and Permission: Literal, Metaphorical, or Deficient?
Analysis of the Hanafi internal dispute on the imperative form's meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on the imperative form for recommendation/permission, explain their proofs, and evaluate the concept of deficient literal (haqiqa qasira).

Core Idea
The imperative form for non-obligatory acts: an internal Hanafi debate between metaphor and deficient literal.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi usul dispute over the linguistic nature of the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) when employed for recommended or permissible acts. The two camps — al-Karkhi/al-Jassas (metaphor) and Fakhr al-Islam al-Pazdawi (deficient literal) — offer competing analyses of Quranic evidence. Understanding this ikhtilaf is key to grasping how Hanafis handle texts that appear to command non-obligatory acts.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the core question
When the imperative form (if‘al) is used for a recommended or permissible act, is it used literally, metaphorically, or in a deficient literal sense?
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Karkhi and al-Jassas hold it is metaphorical (مجاز); Fakhr al-Islam holds it is a deficient literal (حقيقة قاصرة).
Both are Hanafi imams; this is an intra-madhhab dispute.
3
Extract the proof for metaphor
Al-Karkhi and al-Jassas rely on the negation test: one can say 'the nafl is not commanded,' negating the real imperative meaning (obligation). If it were literal, such negation would be impossible.
4
Extract the proof for deficient literal
Fakhr al-Islam uses induction from Quranic verses (e.g., فإذا قضيت الصلاة فانتشروا) to show that the imperative does not by itself indicate permissibility of abandonment; that comes from external context. This is unlike clear metaphors like 'lion' for a brave man.
He argues the form indicates a common meaning (demand), and context specifies the degree.
5
Analyze haqiqa qasira
A deficient literal uses a word for a part of its original meaning. Here, the imperative is put for a general 'demand', and recommendation/permission are partial instances of that general concept.
This is distinct from full literal (whole meaning) and metaphor (outside meaning).
6
Review objections and responses
Objections include the 'lion' counterexample and the incompatibility with al-Karkhi/al-Jassas' proof. Fakhr al-Islam's defenders argue that the Quranic imperatives do not convey permissibility by themselves, unlike genuine metaphors.
7
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
If deficient literal, Quranic/hadith imperatives for non-obligatory acts are considered literal, affecting qiyas and istihsan. If metaphorical, they are figurative and less directly used in analogy.
