الاستقراء الغير المكذوب وكيف يدعي أحد أنه يفهم من كنت نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور فزوروها فإنها تذكرة جواز الترك، بل إنما يفهم ذلك من جهة أخرى، وهذا بخلاف الأسد، فإنه يفهم منه الرجل الشجاع في موارد الاستعمال لا الشجاع المطلق، وكذا يفهم من القمر الإنسان الجميل لا الحسن المطلق فاندفع الأول أيضاً وبما قررنا ظهر لك اندفاع ما قيل يصير النزاع حينئذ لفظياً، لأن الحقيقة القاصرة اصطلاح خاص لا يفهمه الكافة، وهي مجاز باصطلاحهم، فإثباتهم المجازية لا ينفيه الحقيقة القاصرة فتدبر، فقد ظهر لك سر ما قال صدر الشريعة إنه دقيق، ثم بقي ههنا كلام آخر هو أنه هب أن هذه الموارد كذلك لكن لما لم يكن سماع الجزئيات شرطاً في التجوز، وعلاقة التشبيه بينهما موجودة مصححة للاستعارة فإذا استعمل في الندب أو الإباحة بخصوصهما يكون استعارة البتة ولا يتم الكلام إلا أن يثبت المنع من اللغة هذا النحو من التجوز ولم يثبت إلى الآن فتأمل ولعل هذا الحبر الهمام إنما نازع وحكم بكونه حقيقة في الأوامر الواردة في محال الندب أو الإباحة في القرآن والحديث، لا أنه ينكر استعارته لهما مطلقاً فتدبر قال بعض المتأخرين: الذي بلغ مبلغ السابقين في شرح المنار في تقرير كلام الإمام فخر الإسلام أن الأحكام الثلاثة ليست متباينة بالذات، وإنما التفاوت بالاعتبار من جهة الشدة والضعف ضرورة أن الطلب القائم بذاته تعالى أمر واحد لكنه معروض للشدة والضعف والتوسط فهو من جهة الشدة إيجاب ومن جهة التوسط ندب، ومن جهة الضعف إباحة، فالأمر المستعمل في الندب والإباحة ليس مستعملاً في غير الوجوب فلا مجاز ولكن لما غلب استعماله في الطلب اعتبار الشدة حتى صار في العرف إسماً له، قال: إن معنى الإباحة والندب بعضه في التقدير هذا خلاصة كلامه وهو لا يفهم بعد، فإنا سلمنا أن الأحكام الثلاثة متغايرة اعتباراً لكن صيغة الأمر لأي شيء وضعت للطلب مع الذي صار به مغايراً لهما، فإذا استعمل في الندب أو الإباحة يكون مجازاً قطعاً، فإنه استعمل في غير ما وضع له ولو كان مغايراً له بالاعتبار وإن وضعت للطلب المطلق من غير ملاحظة الاعتبار الموجب للمغايرة بين الوجوب وبينهما لم يكن موضوعاً للوجوب وتوقف فهمه على قرينة زائدة فافهم، ولقد أطنبنا الكلام في هذا المقام لما كان من أعضل مشكلات كلام هذا الحبر الهمام قد اعترف بالقصور عن حله كثير من الأئمة الكرام حتى البحر القمقام صاحب الكشف، فعليك بالتأمل الصادق والنظر الفائق ومن الله الاعتصام.
The third article on linguistic principles: Is the recommended (mandub) commanded or not? It is replied that no one said that the permissible (mubah) is commanded except al-Ka'bi from the Mu'tazila, and it follows from that that it is commanded according to the Ahl al-Sunna who suppress innovation. He said in al-Talwih: This direction was good were it not for including the permissible in this category, and it is an error on his part, for Imam Fakhr al-Islam does not consider the recommended to be truly commanded. Also, the inference does not support this direction, which is his statement: 'The meaning of permission and recommendation is part of it' so understand. (And it is said): The dispute is not over the word 'command' but over the imperative form (sigha), and it is literal for obligation in the absence of a context (qarina), and for them (i.e., recommendation and permission) with context, and this does not remove the metaphor (majaz) because the literal is usage in what it was put for to indicate by itself without the addition of a context, whereas metaphor is opposite. (Otherwise it would remove metaphor absolutely because every metaphor is placed for a metaphorical meaning with context as has passed. And the disputant can say: The form is homonymous (mushtaraka) between the three, but one of its meanings, obligation, is immediately understood without context due to predominance of usage, and the other two with context, so the word is literal in them. Thus in al-Hashiya, and in the expression 'disputant' there is an indication of weakness, and its aspect is that Imam Fakhr al-Islam does not affirm homonymy while choosing that it is literal, and his proof does not apply, because its being placed as literal then is not due to partiality, but because it is placed for it then, so understand. (And it is said): The intended meaning of literal and metaphorical is not the well-known one, but the division of the word according to usage is threefold: first, al-haqiqa (literal) which is used in the complete meaning it was put for; second, al-majaz (metaphor) which is used in other than what it was put for, i.e., outside it; and third, al-haqiqa al-qasira (deficient literal) which is used for a part of what it was put for, based on it not being identical (which is apparent) nor fully other. This is the best that has been verified in speech, and it is what is meant by literal here. So the result is that the use of the imperative form for recommendation or permission is either a metaphor by way of applying one of two divergent things to the other due to a common attribute, or a deficient literal by way of applying the whole to the part. So the imperative, when used for them, only indicates permission common to all three, or preference common between obligation and recommendation, and the establishment of what differentiates and the permissibility of abandonment is only by external context. This is more correct according to him. Objections were raised against it: First, what the author indicated by his saying: 'And it is not hidden what weakness is in it, even though it is said in al-Tawdih that it is subtle.' In summary, it necessitates that 'lion' (as in 'a man lion') would not be metaphorical, because it could be claimed that it is also used for absolute bravery, whether lion or human, and understanding what differentiates is by external context. But this is false by consensus, for all agree that it is a metaphor. Second: It does not apply to the inference of the two imams al-Karkhi and al-Jassas, for their inference only establishes the soundness of negating the command [meaning] from it, whereas the dispute is about the form. Third: In the inference for the deficient literal on the basis that it is used for absolute permission or preference, this contradicts the hypothesis and goes beyond the point of dispute, for the dispute is when the form is used and recommendation or permission is intended, whereas here it is not intended but rather a common meaning. The clarification of his statement is that the intended matter is that the command used in contexts of recommendation or permission, like 'When you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt' and 'When the prayer is completed, then disperse' and others, is it a metaphor or a deficient literal? So the third objection is repelled, for he did not assume its use in them such that they are specifically intended, rather he assumed its use in their contexts such that they are understood, even if by way of a common meaning, except that it is a tolerance as is the custom of the mashayikh. So Imam al-Karkhi and Imam al-Jassas went for the first, and the summary of their proof is that the real meaning of the form (obligatory requiring) is negated from them, and they expressed this by 'command', because it is said 'the nafl is not commanded or wajib', i.e., the act is related to 'if'al' used in its real sense. Then the proof applies and the second objection falls. And he himself chose the second, and the summary of his proof is that the forms used in places of recommendation or permission do not convey permissibility of abandonment at all, as the unfalsified induction witnesses. How can anyone claim that from 'I had forbidden you from visiting graves, now visit them, for they are a reminder' one understands permissibility of abandonment? Rather, that is understood from another source. This is unlike 'lion', for one understands from it the brave man in contexts of usage, not absolute bravery. Similarly, 'moon' gives the beautiful human, not absolute beauty. So the first objection is also repelled. And from what we have established, it becomes clear to you the repelling of what was said: that the dispute becomes verbal, because 'deficient literal' is a special technical term not understood by the common people, and it is metaphor in their terminology, so their affirming metaphoricity does not negate deficient literal. So reflect. Thus the secret of what Sadr al-Shari'a said (that it is subtle) becomes clear to you. Then there remains another speech here, which is that granting that these cases are so, but since hearing particulars is not a condition for going into metaphor, and the relation of similitude exists between them, making the metaphor sound, so if it is used for recommendation or permission specifically, it is definitely a metaphor. The discussion cannot be completed unless it is established that the language prohibits this kind of metaphor, and that has not been established yet, so contemplate. Perhaps this illustrious scholar only disputed and ruled that it is literal in the commands used in contexts of recommendation or permission in the Quran and hadith, not that he denies its metaphorization for them absolutely, so contemplate. Some later scholars said: The one who reached the rank of the predecessors in his commentary on al-Manar, in his exposition of Imam Fakhr al-Islam's speech: that the three legal categories (obligation, recommendation, permission) are not essentially distinct, but rather the difference is by consideration of intensity and weakness, necessarily because the demand that subsists in the essence of Allah is one thing but it is subject to intensity, weakness, and intermediateness. So from the perspective of intensity it is obligation, from intermediateness recommendation, from weakness permission. So the command used in recommendation or permission is not used in other than obligation, hence no metaphor. But because its usage for demand with intensity became predominant until it became in custom a name for it, he said: The meaning of permission and recommendation is part of it in estimation. This is the summary of his speech, but it is not understood. Because even if we accept that the three categories differ by consideration, for what was the imperative form put? For the demand with that which makes it different from the other two? If so, when used for recommendation or permission it is definitely metaphor, because it is used in other than what it was put for, even if it differs from them by consideration. And if it was put for absolute demand without noting the consideration that necessitates difference between obligation and them, then it was not put for obligation, and understanding obligation depends on an extra context, so understand. And we have prolonged the discussion in this place because it is one of the most difficult problems in the speech of this illustrious scholar; many great imams have admitted inability to solve it, even the vast ocean, the author of al-Kashshaf. So upon you is sincere reflection and superior consideration, and from Allah is preservation.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe into the Hanafi usul dispute regarding the nature of the imperative form (صيغة الأمر) when used for recommended (مندوب) or permissible (مباح) acts. The central question is whether such usage is literal (حقيقة) or metaphorical (مجاز). The text presents multiple positions.

The first view, attributed to the Mu'tazili al-Ka'bi, holds that the permissible is also commanded. The Ahl al-Sunna reject this. The commentator notes that Imam Fakhr al-Islam (al-Pazdawi) does not consider the recommended to be actually commanded in the literal sense.

A second view states that the dispute is not about the word 'command' but about the imperative form itself. According to this view, the imperative is literal for obligation in the absence of a contextual indicator (قرينة), and metaphorical for recommendation and permission when a context is present. However, the commentator argues that the presence of context does not remove the metaphor, because metaphor is defined by usage with context.

The third view introduces a tripartite division of usage: literal (used for the complete meaning), metaphorical (used for something else), and 'deficient literal' (حقيقة قاصرة) used for a part of the meaning. The author (Fakhr al-Islam) chooses the third view: the imperative used for recommendation/permission is a deficient literal, like using the whole for a part. It indicates a common meaning (permission or preference), and the specific obligation/recommendation distinction comes from external context.

Objections are raised: (1) This would make 'lion' for a brave man not metaphorical, which is against consensus. (2) It does not match the proof of Imams al-Karkhi and al-Jassas, who rely on negation of obligation. (3) It assumes a common meaning, whereas the dispute is about specific meanings.

Imam al-Karkhi and al-Jassas hold the metaphorical view. Their proof: one can say 'the nafl is not commanded', negating the real meaning of obligation. The author (Fakhr al-Islam) holds the deficient literal view. His proof: inductive evidence shows that imperatives in contexts of recommendation/permission (like Quranic verses) do not convey permissibility of abandonment; that understanding comes from elsewhere. This differs from clear metaphors like 'lion' for a brave man, where the specific meaning is directly understood.

The passage concludes with a subtle point: even if the deficient literal is accepted, metaphorical usage is still possible because the condition of analogy is present. The author's claim may be restricted to Quran and hadith, not absolute denial of metaphorical use. Some later scholars attempt to reconcile the positions by considering the three legal categories as differing by degree (intensity, weakness) rather than essence, but the commentator finds this unconvincing because the imperative is placed for one meaning, making its use for another metaphorical.

Key Terms
صيغة الأمر (sīghat al-amr): The imperative form (e.g., if‘al) which is the focus of dispute regarding its literal meaning.
حقيقة قاصرة (ḥaqīqa qāṣira): A deficient literal usage where a word is used for a part of its original meaning, not the whole.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for something other than its original meaning with a contextual indicator.
استعارة (isti‘āra): A kind of metaphor based on resemblance, e.g., calling a brave man a 'lion'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Fakhr al-Islam al-Pazdawi, what is the nature of the imperative form when used for recommendation or permission?
A) It is metaphorical (مجاز)
B) It is a homonym (مشترك) among the three meanings
C) It is a deficient literal (حقيقة قاصرة)
D) It indicates obligation only

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi internal dispute on the imperative form for non-obligatory acts illustrates the depth of usul al-fiqh in determining whether scriptural language is literal or figurative.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
The Imperative After Prohibition: For Permission or Obligation
Determining the legal implication of an imperative that follows a prior prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the imperative after prohibition and evaluate the counter-argument of the majority regarding customary reality.

Core Idea
The imperative after prohibition retains its literal obligation for Hanafis; the majority argues its prevalence in permission creates a customary reality.

When an imperative follows a prior prohibition—either directly or after the removal of the prohibition's cause—scholars differ on its legal implication. The core question: does it indicate permission (ibāḥah) or obligation (wujūb)? This passage from Fawātih al-Raḥamūt presents the dispute between the majority school (including al-Shāfiʿī) and the Ḥanafīs, along with a nuanced critique of the majority's claim that the imperative has become a customary reality for permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key issue
Pinpoint the precise question: what is the ruling (ḥukm) of an imperative that appears after a prohibition? Recognize that the dispute is about whether the imperative is for permission or obligation.
The imperative is considered after the prohibition is lifted.
2
State the opposing positions
List the two main views: the majority (including al-Shāfiʿī and al-Āmidī) holds it indicates permission; the generality of Ḥanafīs holds it indicates obligation. Note that some Shāfiʿīs (al-Bāqillānī, al-Rāzī) also held obligation.
The Ḥanafī position is attributed to most Ḥanafīs.
3
Analyze the evidence for the majority view
Examine the majority's argument: the prevalence of the imperative being used for permission in Islamic law (e.g., 'hunt after pilgrimage', 'disperse after prayer') makes it a customary reality (ḥaqīqah ʿurfiyyah). This reality takes precedence over the literal linguistic meaning.
Customary reality is established when the meaning comes to mind without context.
4
Evaluate the Ḥanafī response
Understand the Ḥanafī counter: the examples cited have contextual indicators (qarāʾin) that direct them to permission; thus, the prevalence is not sufficient to establish a custom. The literal meaning of obligation remains unless a clear diverting proof exists.
The Ḥanafīs cite 'kill the polytheists' (Q 9:5) as an imperative after prohibition that is clearly for obligation.
5
Apply the dispute to a practical example
Consider the Prophetic statement: 'I had forbidden you from visiting graves, so visit them.' For the Ḥanafīs, this indicates obligation (wujūb) to visit graves (with conditions); for the majority, it indicates permission or recommendation.
This example shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf).

Source Text

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية مسألة (صيغة الأمر الواردة (بعد الحظر والتحريم بأن يقع متصلاً به نحو: كنت نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور ألا فزوروها أو يقع معلقاً بزوال سبب التحريم نحو : وإذا حللتم فاصطادوا (للإباحة عند الأكثر ، ومنهم الإمام الشافعي والآمدي وللوجوب عند عامة الحنفية وهو المروي عن القاضي الباقلاني من الشافعية والمعتزلة واختاره الإمام فخر الدين (الرازي) من الشافعية (والأمر بعد الاستئذان كالأمر بعد التحريم) والخلاف في كونه للإباحة أو الوجوب (على ما) نقل (في المحصول وتوقف إمام الحرمين في الواقع بعد الحظر (وقيل) الأمر بعد الحظر (لما طرأ عليه حظر إباحة كان أو وجوباً واختاره الشيخ ابن الهمام (وهو قريب) إلى الصواب للأكثر غلبتها في الإباحة في عرف الشرع غلبة يسرع بها إليه من غير قرينة حتى صارت الحقيقة مهجورة فيقدم على اللغة أي الحقيقة اللغوية (لأنه) أي الاستعمال فيها (مجاز) لأنها غير ما وضع له في العرف وتقديم الحقيقة العرفية على اللغوية بالاتفاق لهجرانها وإنما الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبه في الحقيقة المستعملة المجاز المتعارف فافهم وذلك نحو قوله تعالى : وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَأَصْطَادُوا ﴾ [المائدة: ٢] وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا قُضِيَتِ الصَّلَوةُ فَانتَشِرُوا ﴾ [الجمعة: ١٠] وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم كنت نهيتكم عن إدخار لحوم الأضاحي فوق ثلاث (فادخروها) وفي صحيح مسلم في حديث طويل : كنت نهيتكم عن إدخار لحوم الأضاحي فوق ثلاث فأمسكوها ما يبدو لكم وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «كنت نهيتكم عن زيارة القبور فقد أذن لمحمد في زيارة قبر أمه (فزوروها) فإنها تذكر الآخرة» رواه الترمذي. وفي هذا المثال نظر، فإنه للندب لا للإباحة إلى غير ذلك من الأمثلة نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا حَتَّى يَتَبَيَّنَ لَكُمُ الْخَيْطُ الأَبْيَضُ مِنَ الْخَيْطِ الأَسود ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۷] ونحو: ﴿عَلِمَ اللَّهُ أَنَّكُمْ كُنتُمْ تَختَانُونَ أَنفُسَكُمْ فَتَابَ عَلَيْكُمْ وَعَفَا عَنكُمْ فَالْكَنَ بَشِرُوهُنَّ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٧] (وما قيل) في الجواب الإباحة فيها لدليل صارف عن الوجوب (وهو العلم بأنها شرعت لنا أي لانتفاعنا فإن الاصطياد مثلاً إنما شرع لنا أكله ونتلذذ بأكله قطعاً فلا ينقلب علينا) أي لا ينقلب مضراً بنا بالوجوب علينا حتى يكون تركه موجباً لاستحقاق العقاب (فغير متوجه إذ مقصودهم حمل المشكوك) في أنه للإباحة أم للوجوب لفقدان الدليل على الغالب المتيقن ولو بالدليل) والغالب الإباحة، وهذا غير متوجه، فإن دعوى المستدل كان صيرورة الإباحة حقيقة عرفية لا تثبت إلا إذا صارت بحيث سبقت إليها من غير قرينة فمنع المجيب الفهم من غير قرينة والذي في هذه الأمثلة قرينة فلم يثبت العرف ومطلق الغلبة لا يثبت العرف، بل قصارى أمره التعارف في الجملة والحقيقة مع القرينة قاضية عليه فتدبر (ولو منع حدوث العرف مستنداً بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا انسَلَخَ الأَشْهُرُ الْحُرُمُ فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٥] فإنه للوجوب وقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) حين جاءت فاطمة بنت حبيش إليه صلوات الله عليه وآله وسلم فقالت : يا رسول الله إني امرأة أستحاض فلا أطهر، أفادع الصلاة؟ قال: «لا إنما ذلك عرق وليس بحيض، فإذا أقبلت حيضتك فدعي الصلاة وإذا أدبرت الحيضة فاغسلي عنك الدم ثم صلي) رواه الشيخان مع الاكتفاء بالمضمر في أدبرت فههنا الأمر أيضاً للوجوب (لكان له وجه) فإنه لم يثبت الغلبة الموجبة للعرف فالمرجع إلى الاستقراء فعليك به وتمسك الحنفية بوجود
The third article on linguistic principles. Issue: The imperative form occurring after prohibition and restriction, either directly attached to it as in 'I had forbidden you from visiting graves, so visit them', or suspended on the removal of the cause of prohibition as in 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt', is for permission according to the majority, including Imam al-Shafi'i and al-Amidi, and for obligation according to the generality of the Hanafis, and it is also related from al-Qadi al-Baqillani among the Shafi'is and the Mu'tazila, and chosen by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi from the Shafi'is. And the command after permission is like the command after prohibition in the dispute over whether it indicates permission or obligation, as transmitted in al-Mahsul. Imam al-Haramayn withheld judgement regarding that which occurs after prohibition. And it was said that the command after prohibition, because prohibition came upon it, is either permission or obligation, and this was chosen by Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, and it is closer to correctness. For the majority, its prevalence in permission in the custom of the Law is a prevalence that quickly comes to mind without a context, until the literal meaning became abandoned, so it takes precedence over the linguistic meaning. Because the usage in it is metaphorical, since it is other than what was placed for in custom, and giving precedence to the customary reality over the linguistic is by agreement due to its abandonment. The only dispute between the Imam and his companion is regarding the reality used as a common metaphor. Understand. Examples include: 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt'; 'And when the prayer has been performed, then disperse'; and his saying, peace be upon him: 'I had forbidden you from storing the meat of sacrificial animals for more than three days, so store them.' And in Sahih Muslim, a long hadith: 'I had forbidden you from storing the meat of sacrificial animals for more than three days, so keep them as you wish.' And his saying: 'I had forbidden you from visiting graves, but Muhammad has been given permission to visit his mother's grave, so visit them, for they are a reminder of the Hereafter' - narrated by al-Tirmidhi. And this example is questionable, for it is for recommendation, not permission. And other examples like His saying: 'And eat and drink until the white thread becomes clear to you from the black thread'; and His saying: 'Allah knows that you were deceiving yourselves, so He accepted your repentance and forgave you, so now consort with them.' And what is said in response - that permission is in them due to a proof diverting from obligation, such as the knowledge that they were legislated for us, i.e., for our benefit, for example hunting was only legislated for us to eat and enjoy, so it cannot become harmful to us by being obligatory on us such that leaving it would incur punishment - is not valid, because their aim is to carry the doubtful (as to whether it is for permission or obligation due to lack of proof) to the predominant certain, even if by proof, and the predominant is permission. And this is not valid, for the claim of the arguer that permission became a customary reality cannot be established unless it becomes such that it comes to mind without a context. But the respondent prevents understanding without a context, and in these examples there is a context, so the custom is not established. Mere prevalence does not establish custom; rather, at most it is common familiarity, and the reality with context is decisive over it. So reflect. And if one prevents the emergence of custom based on His saying: 'And when the sacred months have passed, then kill the polytheists' - for it is for obligation - and his saying (when Fatimah bint Hubaysh came to him and said: O Messenger of Allah, I am a woman with irregular bleeding and do not become pure, should I leave prayer? He said: No, that is a vein and not menstruation, so when your menstruation comes, leave prayer, and when it ends, wash the blood from you and then pray) - narrated by the Shaykhayn - and there the command is also for obligation - then he would have a point, because the prevalence that would necessitate custom is not established. So the reference is to induction. Adhere to it. And the Hanafis adhere to the existence...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on the ruling of the imperative that appears after a prohibition. The main issue: when an imperative follows a prior prohibition (either directly or after the removal of the cause of prohibition), does it indicate permission (ibāḥah) or obligation (wujūb)? The author states that the majority, including al-Shafi'i and al-Amidi, hold it indicates permission, while the generality of Hanafis hold it indicates obligation. Some Shafi'is (like al-Baqillani and al-Razi) also hold obligation. The passage then discusses arguments and examples, including the famous hadith about visiting graves and storing meat. The author leans toward the view that the prevalence of permission in legal custom makes the imperative a customary metaphor for permission, so it should be interpreted as permission unless there is a clear indication otherwise. However, the Hanafis argue that the imperative retains its literal meaning of obligation unless there is a clear diverting proof. The passage concludes with a critique of the claim that permission is a customary reality, and suggests that induction (istiqrā') is the proper method to determine the predominant usage.

Key Terms
بعد الحظر (ba‘d al-ḥaẓr): After prohibition; an imperative that follows a prior prohibition.
إباحة (ibāḥah): Permission; a ruling that provides legal option to do or leave an act.
وجوب (wujūb): Obligation; a ruling that requires action with reward for compliance and punishment for omission.
حقيقة عرفية (ḥaqīqah 'urfiyyah): A reality established by custom; a meaning that becomes the prevalent understanding due to common usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what does the imperative 'fazūrū' (visit graves) after the Prophet's statement 'I had forbidden you from visiting graves' indicate?
A) It indicates permission (ibāḥah).
B) It indicates obligation (wujūb).
C) It indicates recommendation (nadb).
D) It indicates absolute permissibility (ibāḥah) for men only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis hold the imperative after prohibition retains its literal obligation; the majority argues prevalence of permission creates a customary reality that overrides the literal meaning.

The Definition of Ijza' and Nature of the Dispute
Defining ijza' and analyzing the nature of the ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two definitions of ijza' and explain why the Hanafi school considers the dispute as real, not verbal.

Core Idea
Ijza' by agreement means compliance; the real dispute is whether it entails dropping of make-up (qada').

This lesson unpacks the opening of the passage, where the author clarifies the two possible definitions of ijza'. The first definition (compliance) is agreed upon; the second definition (dropping of make-up) is where the controversy lies. We will see why the Hanafi scholars insist that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila is substantive, not merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of ijza'
Read the opening lines to find the two definitions: (1) ijza' as compliance (imtithal), which is agreed upon, and (2) ijza' as dropping of qada'. Note the author's phrasing.
The first definition is trivial; the second is the locus of dispute.
2
State the agreed-upon position
Confirm that if ijza' is defined as compliance, then performing the act as required necessarily entails ijza' by unanimous agreement (ittifaqan).
3
State the Mu'tazili position
Cite 'Abd al-Jabbar's claim that ijza' does not entail dropping of qada'. Understand his reasoning: it is not improbable for the Wise to leave the responsibility occupied.
The Mu'tazili view is introduced as a counterpoint.
4
Analyse the claim that the dispute is verbal vs real
The author reports that some consider the dispute merely verbal, but then argues emphatically that it is real, based on the statements of scholars and the self-evident nature of the issue.
The author prefers the real dispute view, attributing the Mu'tazili stance to desire overwhelming reason.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi preference
Recognize that the Hanafi school insists on the substantive nature of the ikhtilaf. This sets the stage for the four proofs that follow in subsequent lessons.
This lesson focuses only on the definitional and meta-dispute aspect.

المطابقة، فإن كان مطابقاً للواقع (فذاك) كافٍ (وإلا وجب القضاء) لأنه لم يؤد المأمور به مع (مسقطاً)
Issue: Does performing the commanded act as required, with its conditions and pillars, necessitate sufficiency (ijza') or not? If sufficiency is interpreted as compliance, then yes, it necessitates sufficiency by agreement, because compliance is performing the commanded act as required, nothing else. If it is defined as the dropping of make-up (qada') from the responsibility actually or virtually, as in the case of 'Eid and similar, then the chosen view among all Usul scholars is that it necessitates it and there is no make-up upon the responsibility. 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili said: It does not necessitate it; there is no improbability on the part of the Wise to leave the responsibility occupied with performing that performed obligatory act and to demand make-up. It was said: His doctrine is that it is not improbable to establish in the responsibility an equivalent of the performed obligatory act and call it make-up. On this, the dispute is verbal, and it is more appropriate because the non-occupation of the responsibility with it after its performance is self-evident, unbecoming for a rational person to utter its denial, let alone adopt it as a doctrine. However, statements of the noble scholars, those of hands and supporters, proclaim with the highest proclamation that the dispute is real, so it is more deserving of acceptance, and the statement of 'Abd al-Jabbar is considered due to the domination of desire over reason. This is our first proof: As I say, there is no remaining of demand (iqtida') after performance in language and custom necessarily in transactions like payment of debts and trusts, so it is not demanded after that in commands related to these transactions, and likewise in other acts of worship. So demand does not remain after performance because the usage is one in all commands. And when demand and requirement do not remain, there is nothing upon the responsibility, so no make-up. The objector may say: Granted that the usages of commands are one, but the non-remaining of requirement in transactions is not due to their being commanded, but for an external reason: that the purpose there is the arrival of wealth once to fulfill his right, unlike acts of worship where the purpose is the act itself, so it may be demanded once after another. Ponder, for it is a place of reflection. And our second proof: If performing it as required does not necessitate dropping of make-up, then compliance would never be known, which is false by agreement. As for the entailment, because its requirement remains after performance; otherwise it would not be a locus for demand of make-up (since there is no dispute about the remaining of requirement by another command), for then it would be an independent obligation, not a make-up of the first. And if the requirement remains, then it was not a performance as required, thus not compliance. The opponent may argue: Just as when not performing as required, make-up is obligatory by the first command, similarly when performing as required, the remaining of requirement in terms of make-up does not negate the knowledge of compliance in the agreed sense, which is performing the commanded act as commanded with its conditions and pillars. So the requirement for the act in terms of performance has dropped, but in terms of make-up it remains. Reflect. And in the wording 'argument' there is indication of the weakness of the objection, which is apparent: he distinguishes between non-performance and performance; there is remaining occupation in the former, so to remove it make-up is obligatory, unlike the latter, for when the demand of performance has dropped and nothing remains in the responsibility, what thing is dropped by make-up? If it is said: This is another occupation that must be discharged, it is an independent obligation, not a make-up. And if it is called make-up, the dispute becomes verbal. Understand. And our third proof: If make-up is not dropped and its demand remains, it would necessitate acquiring the acquired (tahsīl al-hāsil), because the commanded act has been obtained, so what thing is demanded after it? It was said: The second is not the same as the first but its like, so there is no acquiring the acquired. And it was answered: The demanded is the universal nature (al-tabī'a al-kulliyya) of the act, and it has been obtained first by performance, so if it is demanded afterwards, it necessitates demanding the acquisition of what has been acquired, not particularities. I say: The impossibility of acquiring the acquired in universal natures is not granted, for it is not acquisition of that nature by that particular occurrence so as to be impossible, but rather in the form of another individual other than the one performed. Moreover, if what he mentioned were true — that the demanded in command is the universal nature — then make-up would never be realized, because it is an individual of the universal demanded by the command, so it would be a performance of the commanded act. And this addition can be repelled by saying that what is meant by the universal nature is that which occurs in its time, and this restriction does not negate universality. Then the nature occurring outside time is make-up, so there is no acquisition of the acquired. So in the gloss. And it can be established similarly to what passed: that the demanded by the command is the performance of the nature in its time, so when he performs, the obligation drops. If make-up were obligatory, it would necessitate acquiring the acquired, because it is for dropping the first obligation, and it has already dropped by the universal nature once, so dropping again is futile. Ponder. And our fourth proof: Make-up is a redress for what has been missed of the commanded act, but it is assumed that the demanded has been obtained completely and nothing has been missed, so there is no redress, so no make-up. Possibly it is denied that make-up is that — i.e., redress for what is missed. Rather, make-up is performing the like of what was required first by way of necessity. How can it be redress for what is missed for one who obliges it without missing? And you may establish the proof: that performing the like of what was required, if it is for dropping a responsibility that was occupied, then there is no occupation. Occupation occurs only when something is missed; then it becomes a remover. And if it is not for dropping a responsibility, it is an obligation on its own, not a make-up of the first in any way, and if it is called so, the dispute is verbal. And it appears that redress for what is missed is among the necessities of make-up only. So understand. 'Abd al-Jabbar and his followers said: If performing the commanded act as required entailed make-up, then the one who prayed while thinking he was in a state of purity would be sinful and make-up would be dropped from him if it becomes clear that he was in a state of ritual impurity after the time exits. Because if he is commanded to pray with certainty of purity and he did not do so with certainty, then he is sinful because he left the commanded act, which is the first branch. And if conjecture suffices in the commanded prayer, then he has performed as commanded, so make-up drops, which is the second branch. The answer: First, I say: The command is for actual purity. That is, we choose a third branch: that the commanded prayer is with actual purity, but conjecture of its existence is sufficient and its correctness is a proof when it matches reality. If it matches reality, then that suffices; otherwise, make-up is obligatory, because he did not perform the commanded act along with (a remover).
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether performing a commanded act exactly as required (with all conditions and pillars) necessarily entails sufficiency (ijza'), meaning that the obligation is fulfilled and no make-up (qada') is required. The author presents the agreed-upon definition: if ijza' is interpreted as compliance (imtithal), then it is trivially true. The real issue is whether it entails the dropping of make-up. The majority of usul scholars (including Hanafis) affirm that it does, while 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili denies it. The author argues that the dispute is real, not merely verbal, and provides four rational proofs for the Hanafi position. The first proof relies on the linguistic and customary fact that after performing an act, the demand ceases, as seen in transactions; the same applies to acts of worship. The second proof uses a reductio: if performance didn't drop make-up, compliance would never be known, which is absurd. The third proof argues that demanding make-up would entail 'acquiring the acquired' (tahsil al-hasil) because the universal nature of the act has already been obtained. The fourth proof defines make-up as redress for what was missed; here nothing was missed. The objection from 'Abd al-Jabbar concerns the case of a person who prays under a valid conjecture of purity but later discovers he was impure. The Hanafi response distinguishes between actual purity and conjectured purity: the command is for actual purity, but conjecture suffices as a proof. If the conjecture matches reality, the act is valid and no make-up; otherwise, make-up is required because the actual commanded act was not performed. This discussion highlights the interplay between command, performance, and sufficiency in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
الإجزاء (al-ijzā'): Sufficiency: performing an act as commanded such that the obligation is discharged and no make-up is required.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Make-up: performing the same obligation after its time has passed due to a missed performance.
الامتثال (al-imtithāl): Compliance: performing the commanded act exactly as required.
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā'): Requirement or demand: the binding force of the command that calls for the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why do Hanafi scholars insist that the dispute over ijza' is real rather than verbal?
A) Because both sides use the same definition of ijza'.
B) Because the statements of scholars indicate a substantive disagreement with practical implications.
C) Because the Mu'tazila are not rational.
D) Because the disagreement is only about terminology.

Key Takeaway
Ijza' entails dropping of qada' is the Hanafi position; the Mu'tazila's denial is considered a failure of reason, but the dispute is substantive and not merely verbal.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Four Hanafi Rational Proofs for Ijza'
Presenting and debating four rational proofs for the Hanafi position on ijza'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and critically analyze each of the four Hanafi rational proofs that performing the commanded act entails sufficiency.

Core Idea
Four rational proofs establish that make-up cannot be required after perfect compliance: cessation of demand, impossibility of unknown compliance, avoidance of redundant acquisition, and definition of make-up as redress.

After establishing the reality of the dispute, the author presents four rational arguments (adillah aqliyyah) to support the Hanafi position. Each proof is followed by an objection and a response, demonstrating a dialectical method. This lesson walks through each proof, helping you understand not only the arguments but also their logical structure and the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
List the four proofs
Read the passage carefully and enumerate the four proofs: (1) elimination of demand after performance, (2) impossibility of knowing compliance otherwise, (3) impossibility of acquiring the acquired (tahsil al-hasil), (4) definition of qada' as redress for what was missed.
2
Understand the first proof (cessation of demand)
The first proof argues that in language and custom, after an act is performed, the demand (iqtida') ceases, as seen in transactions. The same applies to all commands, including worship. Note the objection that transactions have an external purpose (arrival of wealth) while worship's purpose is the act itself, which may be repeatable.
The author says 'ponder' (تأمل) at this point, indicating a weak objection.
3
Understand the second proof (otherwise compliance unknown)
The second proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: if performing as required did not drop qada', then compliance would never be known with certainty. The objection distinguishes between the demand for performance (which drops) and the demand for qada' (which remains), so certainty is still possible.
The author labels the objection as a 'mujadala' (argumentation) implying weakness.
4
Understand the third proof (tahsil al-hasil)
The third proof argues that demanding a make-up after perfect performance would be demanding what has already been obtained, which is impossible. The objection says the second act is not the same but similar, so it is not tahsil al-hasil. The Hanafi response focuses on the universal nature (tabi'a kulliyya) of the commanded act, which has already been realized.
A further nuance: the universal nature is temporally restricted to its time, so a later performance is a different individual, not tahsil al-hasil.
5
Understand the fourth proof (qada' as redress)
The fourth proof defines qada' as redress for what was missed. Since nothing was missed when the act was perfectly performed, there is no need for qada'. The objection redefines qada' as simply performing a similar act, which the Hanafis argue would either be a separate obligation or only verbal.
The author concludes that redress is a necessary characteristic of qada' only, so the definition stands.

Source Text

مسألة الإتيان بالمأمور به على وجهه كما طلب مع الشرائط والأركان (هل يستلزم الإجزاء) أم لا فإن فسر) الإجزاء بالامتثال فنعم يستلزم الإجزاء (اتفاقاً) لأن الامتثال الإتيان بالمأمور به على وجهه لا غير وإن عرّف بسقوط القضاء) عن الذمة تحقيقاً أو تقديراً كما في العيد ونحوه (فالمختار) عند الأصوليين كلهم أنه يستلزمه ولا قضاء على الذمة (وقال عبد الجبار المعتزلي (لا يستلزمه) ولا بعد من الحكيم أن يبقى الذمة مشغولة بأداء ذلك الواجب المؤدى ويطلب القضاء، وقيل: مذهبه لا بعد أن يثبت في الذمة مثل الواجب المؤدى ويسميه قضاء، على هذا النزاع لفظي، وهو أولى من جهة جهة أن عدم اشتغال الذمة به بعد أدائه بديهي لا يليق بحال عاقل أن يتفوه بإنكاره، فضلاً عن أن يتخذه مذهباً، لكن عبارات العلماء الكرام ذوي الأيدي والأنصار تنادي أعلى نداء يكون النزاع معنوياً، فهو الأحق بالقبول، ويجعل قول عبد الجبار من جهة غلبة الهوى على العقل، هذا لنا :أولاً كما أقول ولا بقاء للاقتضاء بعد الإتيان في اللغة والعرف بالضرورة في المعاملات كأداء الديون والأمانات فلا يطلب بعده في الأوامر المتعلقة بهذه المعاملات فكذا في غيرها من العبادات، فلا يبقى الطلب بعد الإتيان (لأن الوضع واحد في الأوامر كلها وإذا لم يبق الطلب والاقتضاء فلا شيء على الذمة فلا قضاء، وللمناقش أن يقول : هب أن أوضاع الأوامر واحدة لكن عدم بقاء الاقتضاء في المعاملات ليس من جهة كونها مأموراً بها، بل لأمر خارج هو أن المقصود هناك وصول المال مرة جبراً لحقه، ولا كذلك العبادات فإن المقصود هناك الفعل فيجوز أن يطلب مرة بعد أخرى فتأمل فيه، فإنه موضع تأمل (و) لنا :(ثانياً : لو لم يستلزم الإتيان على وجهه سقوط القضاء (لم يعلم امتثال أبداً، وهو باطل اتفاقاً، أما الملازمة فلأن اقتضاءه باق بعد أي بعد الإتيان، وإلا لم يكن محلاً لمطالبة القضاء (إذ لا نزاع في بقاء الاقتضاء بأمر آخر) فإنه يكون واجباً مستقلاً لا قضاء للأول وإذا كان الاقتضاء باقياً فلم يكن إتياناً به على وجهه فلا يكون امتثالاً (وللخصم أن يجادل بأن عند عدم الإتيان به على وجهه، كما يجب القضاء بالأمر الأول، كذلك عند الإتيان به كذلك أي على وجهه فبقاء الاقتضاء بحسب القضاء، لا ينافي العلم بالامتثال بالمعنى المتفق عليه وهو إتيان المأمور به كما أمر مع شرائطه وأركانه فالاقتضاء للفعل بحسب قد سقط وبحسب القضاء باق (تدبر) وفي لفظ المجادلة إشارة إلى ضعف الإيراد وهو ظاهر فإنه فرق بين عدم الإتيان والإتيان فإن اشتغال باقٍ في الأول فلإسقاطه وجب القضاء، بخلاف الثاني، فإنه إذا سقط مطالبة الأداء ولم يبق شيء في الذمة فأي شيء يسقط بالقضاء، وإن قيل : يكون هذا اشتغالاً آخر لا بد من تفريغه فهو واجب مستقل، لا أنه قضاء وإن سمي قضاء صار النزاع لفظياً فافهم (و) لنا :(ثالثاً : لو لم يسقط القضاء وتبقى مطالبته الزم تحصيل الحاصل فإن المأمور به قد حصل فأي شيء يطلب بعده (قيل : الثاني ليس نفس الأول بل مثله فليس هناك تحصيل الحاصل (وأجيب بأن المطلوب الطبيعة الكلية) للفعل وقد حصل أولاً بالإتيان، فلو طلب بعده لزم طلب تحصيل الحاصل (لا) الخصوصيات) أي ليس المطلوب خصوصيات الأفعال حتى يكون الثاني مثل الأول (أقول : استحالة تحصيل الحاصل في الطبائع الكلية ممنوعة فإنه ليس تحصيلاً له بذلك الحصول) حتى يكون محالاً (بل في ضمن فرد آخر غير المأتي به على أنه لو تم ما ذكره من أن المطلوب في الأمر الطبيعة الكلية (لم يتحقق القضاء أصلاً، لأنه فرد من أفراد الكلي المطلوب بالأمر، فيكون إتياناً بالمأمور به، ويمكن دفع هذه العلاوة بأن المراد بالطبيعة الكلية هي الحاصلة في وقتها، وهذا التقييد لا ينافي الكلية، وحينئذ فالطبيعة الحاصلة خارج الوقت قضاء، فلا تحصيل للحاصل، كذا في الحاشية ؛ ويمكن أن يقرر الكلام بمثل ما مر بأن المطلوب بالأمر إتيان الطبيعة في وقتها فإذا أتى فقد سقط العهدة، فلو وجب القضاء لزم تحصيل الحاصل فإنه لإسقاط العهدة الأولى وهو قد سقط بالطبيعة الكلية مرة، فالإسقاط مرة أخرى لغو فتدبر (و) لنا ( رابعاً: القضاء استدراك لما فات المأمور به والمفروض أنه حصل المطلوب بتمامه ولم يفت منه شيء) فلا من استدراك فلا قضاء (وربما يمنع أن القضاء ذلك) أي استدراك ما فات (بل القضاء الإتيان بمثل ما وجب أولاً بطريق اللزوم وكيف يكون استدراكاً لما فات عند من يوجبه من غير فوت، ولك أن تقرر الدليل بأن إتيان مثل ما وجب إن كان لإسقاط ذمة كانت مشغولة فلا شغل، إنما الشغل إذا فات منه شيء فحينئذ يكون مسقطاً، وإن لم يكن لإسقاط ذمة فهو واجب برأسه لا قضاء الأول في شيء، وإن سمي به فالنزاع لفظي، وظهر أن استدراك ما فات من لوازم الاقضاء فقط فافهم عبد الجبار وأتباعه قالوا: لو كان الإتيان بالمأمور به على وجهه له أي القضاء لكان المصلي بظن الطهارة آثماً وساقطاً عنه القضاء إذا تبين الحدث بعد خروج الوقت لأنه إن أمر بها أي بالصلاة بيقين الطهارة فلم يفعل مع اليقين بها (فيأثم) لأنه ترك المأمور به وهو الشق الأول (وإن كفى الظن في الصلاة المأمور بها (فقد أتى كما أمر فيسقط) القضاء وهو الشق الثاني (والجواب :أولاً: أقول : الأمر بالطهارة الواقعية) أي نختار شقاً ثالثاً هو أن المأمور بها الصلاة الطهارة الواقعية لكن الظن بقيامها كافي وصحة الظن لأنه دليل مع المطابقة، فإن كان مطابقاً للواقع (فذاك) كافٍ (وإلا وجب القضاء) لأنه لم يؤد المأمور به مع (مسقطاً)
Issue: Does performing the commanded act as required, with its conditions and pillars, necessitate sufficiency (ijza') or not? If sufficiency is interpreted as compliance, then yes, it necessitates sufficiency by agreement, because compliance is performing the commanded act as required, nothing else. If it is defined as the dropping of make-up (qada') from the responsibility actually or virtually, as in the case of 'Eid and similar, then the chosen view among all Usul scholars is that it necessitates it and there is no make-up upon the responsibility. 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili said: It does not necessitate it; there is no improbability on the part of the Wise to leave the responsibility occupied with performing that performed obligatory act and to demand make-up. It was said: His doctrine is that it is not improbable to establish in the responsibility an equivalent of the performed obligatory act and call it make-up. On this, the dispute is verbal, and it is more appropriate because the non-occupation of the responsibility with it after its performance is self-evident, unbecoming for a rational person to utter its denial, let alone adopt it as a doctrine. However, statements of the noble scholars, those of hands and supporters, proclaim with the highest proclamation that the dispute is real, so it is more deserving of acceptance, and the statement of 'Abd al-Jabbar is considered due to the domination of desire over reason. This is our first proof: As I say, there is no remaining of demand (iqtida') after performance in language and custom necessarily in transactions like payment of debts and trusts, so it is not demanded after that in commands related to these transactions, and likewise in other acts of worship. So demand does not remain after performance because the usage is one in all commands. And when demand and requirement do not remain, there is nothing upon the responsibility, so no make-up. The objector may say: Granted that the usages of commands are one, but the non-remaining of requirement in transactions is not due to their being commanded, but for an external reason: that the purpose there is the arrival of wealth once to fulfill his right, unlike acts of worship where the purpose is the act itself, so it may be demanded once after another. Ponder, for it is a place of reflection. And our second proof: If performing it as required does not necessitate dropping of make-up, then compliance would never be known, which is false by agreement. As for the entailment, because its requirement remains after performance; otherwise it would not be a locus for demand of make-up (since there is no dispute about the remaining of requirement by another command), for then it would be an independent obligation, not a make-up of the first. And if the requirement remains, then it was not a performance as required, thus not compliance. The opponent may argue: Just as when not performing as required, make-up is obligatory by the first command, similarly when performing as required, the remaining of requirement in terms of make-up does not negate the knowledge of compliance in the agreed sense, which is performing the commanded act as commanded with its conditions and pillars. So the requirement for the act in terms of performance has dropped, but in terms of make-up it remains. Reflect. And in the wording 'argument' there is indication of the weakness of the objection, which is apparent: he distinguishes between non-performance and performance; there is remaining occupation in the former, so to remove it make-up is obligatory, unlike the latter, for when the demand of performance has dropped and nothing remains in the responsibility, what thing is dropped by make-up? If it is said: This is another occupation that must be discharged, it is an independent obligation, not a make-up. And if it is called make-up, the dispute becomes verbal. Understand. And our third proof: If make-up is not dropped and its demand remains, it would necessitate acquiring the acquired (tahsīl al-hāsil), because the commanded act has been obtained, so what thing is demanded after it? It was said: The second is not the same as the first but its like, so there is no acquiring the acquired. And it was answered: The demanded is the universal nature (al-tabī'a al-kulliyya) of the act, and it has been obtained first by performance, so if it is demanded afterwards, it necessitates demanding the acquisition of what has been acquired, not particularities. I say: The impossibility of acquiring the acquired in universal natures is not granted, for it is not acquisition of that nature by that particular occurrence so as to be impossible, but rather in the form of another individual other than the one performed. Moreover, if what he mentioned were true — that the demanded in command is the universal nature — then make-up would never be realized, because it is an individual of the universal demanded by the command, so it would be a performance of the commanded act. And this addition can be repelled by saying that what is meant by the universal nature is that which occurs in its time, and this restriction does not negate universality. Then the nature occurring outside time is make-up, so there is no acquisition of the acquired. So in the gloss. And it can be established similarly to what passed: that the demanded by the command is the performance of the nature in its time, so when he performs, the obligation drops. If make-up were obligatory, it would necessitate acquiring the acquired, because it is for dropping the first obligation, and it has already dropped by the universal nature once, so dropping again is futile. Ponder. And our fourth proof: Make-up is a redress for what has been missed of the commanded act, but it is assumed that the demanded has been obtained completely and nothing has been missed, so there is no redress, so no make-up. Possibly it is denied that make-up is that — i.e., redress for what is missed. Rather, make-up is performing the like of what was required first by way of necessity. How can it be redress for what is missed for one who obliges it without missing? And you may establish the proof: that performing the like of what was required, if it is for dropping a responsibility that was occupied, then there is no occupation. Occupation occurs only when something is missed; then it becomes a remover. And if it is not for dropping a responsibility, it is an obligation on its own, not a make-up of the first in any way, and if it is called so, the dispute is verbal. And it appears that redress for what is missed is among the necessities of make-up only. So understand. 'Abd al-Jabbar and his followers said: If performing the commanded act as required entailed make-up, then the one who prayed while thinking he was in a state of purity would be sinful and make-up would be dropped from him if it becomes clear that he was in a state of ritual impurity after the time exits. Because if he is commanded to pray with certainty of purity and he did not do so with certainty, then he is sinful because he left the commanded act, which is the first branch. And if conjecture suffices in the commanded prayer, then he has performed as commanded, so make-up drops, which is the second branch. The answer: First, I say: The command is for actual purity. That is, we choose a third branch: that the commanded prayer is with actual purity, but conjecture of its existence is sufficient and its correctness is a proof when it matches reality. If it matches reality, then that suffices; otherwise, make-up is obligatory, because he did not perform the commanded act along with (a remover).
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether performing a commanded act exactly as required (with all conditions and pillars) necessarily entails sufficiency (ijza'), meaning that the obligation is fulfilled and no make-up (qada') is required. The author presents the agreed-upon definition: if ijza' is interpreted as compliance (imtithal), then it is trivially true. The real issue is whether it entails the dropping of make-up. The majority of usul scholars (including Hanafis) affirm that it does, while 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili denies it. The author argues that the dispute is real, not merely verbal, and provides four rational proofs for the Hanafi position. The first proof relies on the linguistic and customary fact that after performing an act, the demand ceases, as seen in transactions; the same applies to acts of worship. The second proof uses a reductio: if performance didn't drop make-up, compliance would never be known, which is absurd. The third proof argues that demanding make-up would entail 'acquiring the acquired' (tahsil al-hasil) because the universal nature of the act has already been obtained. The fourth proof defines make-up as redress for what was missed; here nothing was missed. The objection from 'Abd al-Jabbar concerns the case of a person who prays under a valid conjecture of purity but later discovers he was impure. The Hanafi response distinguishes between actual purity and conjectured purity: the command is for actual purity, but conjecture suffices as a proof. If the conjecture matches reality, the act is valid and no make-up; otherwise, make-up is required because the actual commanded act was not performed. This discussion highlights the interplay between command, performance, and sufficiency in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
تحصيل الحاصل (tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil): Acquiring the already acquired: a logical impossibility of demanding something that already exists.
الطبيعة الكلية (al-ṭabī'a al-kulliyya): Universal nature: the essential quality of an act that is the object of the command, as opposed to particular instances.
الاستدراك (al-istidrāk): Redress: making up for what was missed. Key to the fourth proof's definition of qada'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which proof argues that demanding make-up after perfect performance would be logically impossible because it would require acquiring what has already been acquired?
A) The first proof
B) The second proof
C) The third proof
D) The fourth proof

Key Takeaway
The four rational proofs collectively demonstrate that performing the commanded act as required logically necessitates the dropping of make-up (qada'), forming the foundation of the Hanafi position on ijza'.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
The Prayer Example: Applying the Ijza' Theory to Conjectured Purity
Applying the theory of ijza' to a concrete fiqh example about prayer and purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi theory of ijza' to the case of prayer performed under valid conjecture of purity, distinguishing when make-up is required.

Core Idea
Actual purity is the commanded condition, but conjecture suffices as proof; if reality contradicts, make-up is due because the actual condition was not met.

The final part of the passage presents a concrete example from 'Abd al-Jabbar to challenge the Hanafi position: a person prays under a valid conjecture of purity, but later discovers he was actually impure. The Mu'tazili uses this to argue that ijza' does not drop qada'. The Hanafi response clarifies that the command is for actual purity, not conjectured purity. This lesson unpacks that response and shows how the theory applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Mu'tazili objection
Read the objection: 'Abd al-Jabbar argues that if ijza' entailed dropping of qada', then the one who prayed under conjecture would either be sinful (if the command was for certain purity) or have no make-up (if conjecture sufficed). Either way, the Hanafi view is contradicted.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Identify the two branches of the objection
The objection presents two branches: (1) If the command is for prayer with certain purity, then the person is sinful because he did not have certainty. (2) If the command is for prayer with conjectured purity, then he performed as commanded and no make-up is needed, which the Mu'tazila claim the Hanafis deny.
3
Present the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response introduces a third branch: the command is for actual purity (tahara waqi'iyya), but conjecture (zann) is a sufficient proof (daili) for the validity of the act. The correctness of the conjecture determines the outcome.
This avoids the dilemma.
4
Apply the response to the two scenarios
If the conjecture matches reality (i.e., the person was actually pure), the prayer is valid and no make-up. If it does not match (he was actually impure), then the actual commanded act was not performed, so make-up is required.
This is consistent with the Hanafi definition of ijza'.
5
Explain why this does not refute the Hanafi position
The example does not show that perfect performance fails to entail sufficiency; it shows that the performance was not perfect because the actual condition (purity) was missing. The conjecture was merely a proof (daili), not the commanded condition itself.
Thus, the Hanafi position remains intact.

(تدبر) وفي لفظ المجادلة إشارة إلى ضعف الإيراد وهو ظاهر فإنه فرق بين عدم الإتيان والإتيان فإن اشتغال باقٍ في الأول فلإسقاطه وجب القضاء، بخلاف الثاني، فإنه إذا سقط مطالبة الأداء ولم يبق شيء في الذمة فأي شيء يسقط بالقضاء، وإن قيل : يكون هذا اشتغالاً آخر لا بد من تفريغه فهو واجب مستقل، لا أنه قضاء وإن سمي قضاء صار النزاع لفظياً فافهم (و) لنا :(ثالثاً : لو لم يسقط القضاء وتبقى مطالبته الزم تحصيل الحاصل فإن المأمور به قد حصل فأي شيء يطلب بعده (قيل : الثاني ليس نفس الأول بل مثله فليس هناك تحصيل الحاصل (وأجيب بأن المطلوب الطبيعة الكلية) للفعل وقد حصل أولاً بالإتيان، فلو طلب بعده لزم طلب تحصيل الحاصل (لا) الخصوصيات) أي ليس المطلوب خصوصيات الأفعال حتى يكون الثاني مثل الأول (أقول : استحالة تحصيل الحاصل في الطبائع الكلية ممنوعة فإنه ليس تحصيلاً له بذلك الحصول) حتى يكون محالاً (بل في ضمن فرد آخر غير المأتي به على أنه لو تم ما ذكره من أن المطلوب في الأمر الطبيعة الكلية (لم يتحقق القضاء أصلاً، لأنه فرد من أفراد الكلي المطلوب بالأمر، فيكون إتياناً بالمأمور به، ويمكن دفع هذه العلاوة بأن المراد بالطبيعة الكلية هي الحاصلة في وقتها، وهذا التقييد لا ينافي الكلية، وحينئذ فالطبيعة الحاصلة خارج الوقت قضاء، فلا تحصيل للحاصل، كذا في الحاشية ؛ ويمكن أن يقرر الكلام بمثل ما مر بأن المطلوب بالأمر إتيان الطبيعة في وقتها فإذا أتى فقد سقط العهدة، فلو وجب القضاء لزم تحصيل الحاصل فإنه لإسقاط العهدة الأولى وهو قد سقط بالطبيعة الكلية مرة، فالإسقاط مرة أخرى لغو فتدبر (و) لنا ( رابعاً: القضاء استدراك لما فات المأمور به والمفروض أنه حصل المطلوب بتمامه ولم يفت منه شيء) فلا من استدراك فلا قضاء (وربما يمنع أن القضاء ذلك) أي استدراك ما فات (بل القضاء الإتيان بمثل ما وجب أولاً بطريق اللزوم وكيف يكون استدراكاً لما فات عند من يوجبه من غير فوت، ولك أن تقرر الدليل بأن إتيان مثل ما وجب إن كان لإسقاط ذمة كانت مشغولة فلا شغل، إنما الشغل إذا فات منه شيء فحينئذ يكون مسقطاً، وإن لم يكن لإسقاط ذمة فهو واجب برأسه لا قضاء الأول في شيء، وإن سمي به فالنزاع لفظي، وظهر أن استدراك ما فات من لوازم الاقضاء فقط فافهم عبد الجبار وأتباعه قالوا: لو كان الإتيان بالمأمور به على وجهه له أي القضاء لكان المصلي بظن الطهارة آثماً وساقطاً عنه القضاء إذا تبين الحدث بعد خروج الوقت لأنه إن أمر بها أي بالصلاة بيقين الطهارة فلم يفعل مع اليقين بها (فيأثم) لأنه ترك المأمور به وهو الشق الأول (وإن كفى الظن في الصلاة المأمور بها (فقد أتى كما أمر فيسقط) القضاء وهو الشق الثاني (والجواب :أولاً: أقول : الأمر بالطهارة الواقعية) أي نختار شقاً ثالثاً هو أن المأمور بها الصلاة الطهارة الواقعية لكن الظن بقيامها كافي وصحة الظن لأنه دليل مع المطابقة، فإن كان مطابقاً للواقع (فذاك) كافٍ (وإلا وجب القضاء) لأنه لم يؤد المأمور به مع (مسقطاً)
Issue: Does performing the commanded act as required, with its conditions and pillars, necessitate sufficiency (ijza') or not? If sufficiency is interpreted as compliance, then yes, it necessitates sufficiency by agreement, because compliance is performing the commanded act as required, nothing else. If it is defined as the dropping of make-up (qada') from the responsibility actually or virtually, as in the case of 'Eid and similar, then the chosen view among all Usul scholars is that it necessitates it and there is no make-up upon the responsibility. 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili said: It does not necessitate it; there is no improbability on the part of the Wise to leave the responsibility occupied with performing that performed obligatory act and to demand make-up. It was said: His doctrine is that it is not improbable to establish in the responsibility an equivalent of the performed obligatory act and call it make-up. On this, the dispute is verbal, and it is more appropriate because the non-occupation of the responsibility with it after its performance is self-evident, unbecoming for a rational person to utter its denial, let alone adopt it as a doctrine. However, statements of the noble scholars, those of hands and supporters, proclaim with the highest proclamation that the dispute is real, so it is more deserving of acceptance, and the statement of 'Abd al-Jabbar is considered due to the domination of desire over reason. This is our first proof: As I say, there is no remaining of demand (iqtida') after performance in language and custom necessarily in transactions like payment of debts and trusts, so it is not demanded after that in commands related to these transactions, and likewise in other acts of worship. So demand does not remain after performance because the usage is one in all commands. And when demand and requirement do not remain, there is nothing upon the responsibility, so no make-up. The objector may say: Granted that the usages of commands are one, but the non-remaining of requirement in transactions is not due to their being commanded, but for an external reason: that the purpose there is the arrival of wealth once to fulfill his right, unlike acts of worship where the purpose is the act itself, so it may be demanded once after another. Ponder, for it is a place of reflection. And our second proof: If performing it as required does not necessitate dropping of make-up, then compliance would never be known, which is false by agreement. As for the entailment, because its requirement remains after performance; otherwise it would not be a locus for demand of make-up (since there is no dispute about the remaining of requirement by another command), for then it would be an independent obligation, not a make-up of the first. And if the requirement remains, then it was not a performance as required, thus not compliance. The opponent may argue: Just as when not performing as required, make-up is obligatory by the first command, similarly when performing as required, the remaining of requirement in terms of make-up does not negate the knowledge of compliance in the agreed sense, which is performing the commanded act as commanded with its conditions and pillars. So the requirement for the act in terms of performance has dropped, but in terms of make-up it remains. Reflect. And in the wording 'argument' there is indication of the weakness of the objection, which is apparent: he distinguishes between non-performance and performance; there is remaining occupation in the former, so to remove it make-up is obligatory, unlike the latter, for when the demand of performance has dropped and nothing remains in the responsibility, what thing is dropped by make-up? If it is said: This is another occupation that must be discharged, it is an independent obligation, not a make-up. And if it is called make-up, the dispute becomes verbal. Understand. And our third proof: If make-up is not dropped and its demand remains, it would necessitate acquiring the acquired (tahsīl al-hāsil), because the commanded act has been obtained, so what thing is demanded after it? It was said: The second is not the same as the first but its like, so there is no acquiring the acquired. And it was answered: The demanded is the universal nature (al-tabī'a al-kulliyya) of the act, and it has been obtained first by performance, so if it is demanded afterwards, it necessitates demanding the acquisition of what has been acquired, not particularities. I say: The impossibility of acquiring the acquired in universal natures is not granted, for it is not acquisition of that nature by that particular occurrence so as to be impossible, but rather in the form of another individual other than the one performed. Moreover, if what he mentioned were true — that the demanded in command is the universal nature — then make-up would never be realized, because it is an individual of the universal demanded by the command, so it would be a performance of the commanded act. And this addition can be repelled by saying that what is meant by the universal nature is that which occurs in its time, and this restriction does not negate universality. Then the nature occurring outside time is make-up, so there is no acquisition of the acquired. So in the gloss. And it can be established similarly to what passed: that the demanded by the command is the performance of the nature in its time, so when he performs, the obligation drops. If make-up were obligatory, it would necessitate acquiring the acquired, because it is for dropping the first obligation, and it has already dropped by the universal nature once, so dropping again is futile. Ponder. And our fourth proof: Make-up is a redress for what has been missed of the commanded act, but it is assumed that the demanded has been obtained completely and nothing has been missed, so there is no redress, so no make-up. Possibly it is denied that make-up is that — i.e., redress for what is missed. Rather, make-up is performing the like of what was required first by way of necessity. How can it be redress for what is missed for one who obliges it without missing? And you may establish the proof: that performing the like of what was required, if it is for dropping a responsibility that was occupied, then there is no occupation. Occupation occurs only when something is missed; then it becomes a remover. And if it is not for dropping a responsibility, it is an obligation on its own, not a make-up of the first in any way, and if it is called so, the dispute is verbal. And it appears that redress for what is missed is among the necessities of make-up only. So understand. 'Abd al-Jabbar and his followers said: If performing the commanded act as required entailed make-up, then the one who prayed while thinking he was in a state of purity would be sinful and make-up would be dropped from him if it becomes clear that he was in a state of ritual impurity after the time exits. Because if he is commanded to pray with certainty of purity and he did not do so with certainty, then he is sinful because he left the commanded act, which is the first branch. And if conjecture suffices in the commanded prayer, then he has performed as commanded, so make-up drops, which is the second branch. The answer: First, I say: The command is for actual purity. That is, we choose a third branch: that the commanded prayer is with actual purity, but conjecture of its existence is sufficient and its correctness is a proof when it matches reality. If it matches reality, then that suffices; otherwise, make-up is obligatory, because he did not perform the commanded act along with (a remover).
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether performing a commanded act exactly as required (with all conditions and pillars) necessarily entails sufficiency (ijza'), meaning that the obligation is fulfilled and no make-up (qada') is required. The author presents the agreed-upon definition: if ijza' is interpreted as compliance (imtithal), then it is trivially true. The real issue is whether it entails the dropping of make-up. The majority of usul scholars (including Hanafis) affirm that it does, while 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili denies it. The author argues that the dispute is real, not merely verbal, and provides four rational proofs for the Hanafi position. The first proof relies on the linguistic and customary fact that after performing an act, the demand ceases, as seen in transactions; the same applies to acts of worship. The second proof uses a reductio: if performance didn't drop make-up, compliance would never be known, which is absurd. The third proof argues that demanding make-up would entail 'acquiring the acquired' (tahsil al-hasil) because the universal nature of the act has already been obtained. The fourth proof defines make-up as redress for what was missed; here nothing was missed. The objection from 'Abd al-Jabbar concerns the case of a person who prays under a valid conjecture of purity but later discovers he was impure. The Hanafi response distinguishes between actual purity and conjectured purity: the command is for actual purity, but conjecture suffices as a proof. If the conjecture matches reality, the act is valid and no make-up; otherwise, make-up is required because the actual commanded act was not performed. This discussion highlights the interplay between command, performance, and sufficiency in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
الظن (al-ẓann): Conjecture: a preponderant belief that falls short of certainty. In usul, it can serve as a valid proof for acts.
الدليل (al-dalīl): Proof: evidence that indicates the presence of a condition or the correctness of an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, what is the commanded condition for the prayer of the person using conjecture?
A) Conjectured purity
B) Certain purity
C) Actual purity
D) Both actual and conjectured purity

Key Takeaway
The prayer example illustrates that in Hanafi usul, ijza' only occurs when the actual commanded conditions are met; valid conjecture suffices as a proof but does not replace the condition itself.
